"Oh, sons, since I have fulfilled for you this book, which is the last of the science of medicine, to complete it and its consequences, and by its explanations and declarations, it seems to me that I accomplish that for you with this treatise, which deals with the operation by hand, that is surgery."
For the operation by hand is greatly hindered in our region and time, so that few know its science, and its traces are almost lost; indeed, there remain only a few descriptions of it in the books of the Ancients, which have been obscured over time, their meanings changed, and they have fallen into error and obscurity, such that their intentions are hidden and their strength is weakened."
"And it seems to me that I revive this with the ordering of this treatise according to the way of exposition and declaration and abbreviation; and also adding illustrations of the shapes of the instruments for cauterization and other tools necessary for the work, and preparing them, which is necessary."
"For the reason why there are not many good practitioners of this craft in our time is because the art of medicine requires that its practitioner be skilled in anatomy beforehand, knowing the arrangement of limbs and their forms, connections and separations; and having knowledge of bones, nerves and ligaments – their number and function; also of the pulsating veins and quiet ones, and the places where they discharge."
"And therefore said Hippocrates with his name, but in practice little, and especially in the art of surgery."
"But we have already said at the beginning of this book that whoever does not know what we have said about anatomy will surely fall into error and cause many deaths of men, as I have seen too often."
"For I saw a foolish and inexperienced surgeon open an escrofulous swelling on the neck of a woman, and cut some arteries in the neck so that much blood flowed out, causing the woman to die immediately."
"And I saw another surgeon who took it upon himself to remove a stone from the bladder of a man who had fallen into that state, and the stone was large; he came near him and removed that stone with a piece of the bladder, so that the man died on the third day."
"And I had already foreseen that outcome and saw that because of the size of the stone and the condition of the patient's illness, he would surely die."
"And I saw another surgeon who was paid a fee of one ducat by our lord to perform surgery, and he undertook the case of a black man who had a fracture of his thighbone; preparing himself with great ignorance, he reduced the fracture without leaving any space, with pillows and stones under strong pressure, and did not allow space for respiration."
"Then he bound him up according to his desires, then released him and let him lie down again for some days, commanding that they release the bandage; and so it happened that the thighbone became inflamed and putrefied, and he came to destruction."
"And I was called to him, and prepared to remove the bandage; but as soon as it was removed, calmness returned, and his pain ceased immediately."
"But nevertheless the corruption was already confirmed in the limb, so that I could not prevent it, and the corruption did not cease to wander throughout the limb until the patient perished."
"And I saw another surgeon who perforated a cancerous swelling, and it became ulcerated in a few days, so that the evil was increased by its lord."
"The reason for this is because the cancerous ulcer is full of melancholic humor, therefore it should not be cauterized with iron on all sides, but only on the part that suffers from inflammation."
"And therefore, children, it is necessary for you that the operation with the hand be divided into two parts: on the one hand is the operation associated with health, and on the other hand is the work where there is danger in the greatest dispositions."
"And I have already spoken about this throughout the whole of this book where operation is associated with error and fear; therefore it is necessary for you to keep this in mind and leave that alone, so that foolish men may not find an opportunity to speak and dishonor you."
"Therefore take up your arms with diligence and protection from God, and treat your illnesses with skillfulness and firmness."
"And use the best way through the grace of God to obtain health and praiseworthy success, leave behind terrible illnesses of difficult healing, show your arms against what makes you fearful, so that steadfastness in your faith and in your name does not desert you: for greater is remaining in your glory, and then in the world and finally in your sanctity."
"Since I have already spoken in some of my books:" "No medical illness, so that bad doctors are not appointed."
"And I have already divided this book according to three chapters."
"Here begins the division of the book, first of the first part." "The first chapter ordered concerning the head, in the forms of instruments and irons for cauterization, and what is necessary in the operation."
"Here begins the second part." "The second chapter concerning cautery and perforation, and cupping glasses, compresses, and extraction of arrows and similar things, all ordered according to the forms of instruments."
"The third chapter concerning reduction and dislocation, and the cure of torticollis and similar things; this chapter is ordered according to the head, in the forms of instruments."
"It is necessary for me to speak about the qualities of those caustics and their effects, and in what complexion cautery is prohibited."
"Therefore I say that the discussion about the quality of cautery and its effects is long and prolix, subtle in science, and secretly hidden; many wise men have already spoken about it, and they disagree among themselves, but for me it suffices to touch upon some of their discussions briefly, fearing prolivity."
"And therefore I say that cautery agrees with every universal complexion, whether it has matter or not, except for two complexions, which are dry without matter."
"In the case of a dry and hot complexion that is out of balance; some say that cautery is beneficial for this, while others say the opposite, that cautery is not suitable for an illness that is due to heat and dryness, as fire itself is hot and dry, and it is inappropriate to use something hot and dry to treat a condition that is already hot and dry."
"And he who says the opposite, that cautery is beneficial in all hot and dry illnesses that occur in men's bodies, argues that when you compare the body of man and its humidity to the nature of fire, you find them alike."
"And I say, based on what that person says, that I have already experienced this many times."
"But it is not necessary for me to repeat what has already been said and practiced in the chapter on cautery through recent exercises, having learned about the diversity of men's complexions and the disposition of illnesses in their bodies, as well as their causes, accidents, and duration."
"But in other complexions, there is less fear for you, especially in hot and humid illnesses, because all physicians agree on these matters and are not disagreed on the use of cautery in them."
"And know, son, that the secret of cure by cautery with fire and its benefit is about cautery in medicine that burns, because fire is simple, whose action only extends to the member being cauterized, and it does not extend to another adjacent member except with some damage."
"But the action of burning medicine extends to those who are near it and often causes difficult-to-heal illnesses in nearby members."
"But fire, due to its sublimity and good substance, only serves to superfluously remove excesses. And we are already familiar with this through experimentation and the length of our service studying this art, which reveals the truth of these causes. Therefore, I am excused from speaking at length on this topic. But if it were not forbidden to me by law in this passage to reveal this secret about fire, and its qualities and operation within bodies and its expulsion of illnesses through philosophical demonstration, which would illuminate your minds."
"And know, son, that they disagree about the time when it is convenient to perform cautery and prefer the best time of spring."
"I say that cautery is convenient at all times, because the damage caused by accidental timing is submerged in the benefit of what is drawn out by cautery. And especially if the cautery is performed for pains or necessary remedies for severe and persistent pains, which cannot tolerate delay, because it is feared that something worse will come about, which is greater than the small damage caused by time."
"And do not think this in your minds, children, for only the common people and foolish doctors estimate that those illnesses cured by cautery are completely incurable at all times, and they put up with that consequence."
"And the reason is not as they think for this, because cautery is only a substitute for medicine that alters complexion and dries out the moistures which are the cause of pains."
"But cautery is superfluous in addition to medicine due to the willingness of its value and virtue of its operation, as well as the greatness of its power."
"But it is possible that its illness will return at some time due to the complexion of the sick person and the location of their illness, as well as the virtue of cautery and what has been prepared in the body from the aggregation of excesses within it, and what was acquired by it in nutrition and similar causes. But the illness in which cautery is administered is one that is subtle in a member with few superfluities and moistures, such as toothache for pain and similar things, it is possible that this pain will not return, but this happens rarely."
"And the common saying is that cautery is the last resort of medicine, and this saying is quite true, because they believe that there is nothing beneficial in it beyond cautery itself."
"And the reason is quite contrary to this; and the intention that cautery is the last resort of medicine means only when one uses the methods of treatment in certain illnesses, and those medicines do not help. And therefore, cautery is administered at the end of the disease, and it proves beneficial." "And hereafter let it be understood that cautery is the last resort of medicine, but not in the sense that vulgar people and many foolish doctors understand it."
"And the ancients said that cautery with gold is better than cautery with iron, and they said this not because of any other reason than the equality of gold and the sublimity of its substance."
"And they said that the location of cautery does not make a scar, which is not absolutely true, because I have already seen and proven that this is only the case in certain bodies without others, and cautery with gold is good and better than with iron as they said; but however when you perform cautery with fire using gold, it is not declared to you, because it depends on the quantity of gold you want due to its luster, and because it prepares to receive friction. And if you adjust the ignition above it, the gold will melt from the fire, and some sheets may fall into the fire due to the occupation of that."
"And for this reason, cautery should be performed with iron more lightly and more carefully in terms of the correctness of its operation."
"And I have already ordered this chapter on cautery according to chapters, and I have ordered those of the head among them for now. So that later when someone asks about something related to it, they may find what they want."
The first chapter
This cauterization confers upon the humid and cold nature of the servant's accident. Its two causes are coldness and great quantity of fluxes from the head to the parts of the eyes and ears, flowing abundantly, causing much sleepiness, toothache, and headache; universally it causes all illnesses that come from coldness, such as paralysis, sodden feet, apoplexy, and similar diseases.
The manner of this cauterization is as follows: firstly, you shall command the patient to be evacuated with a laxative medicine for three or four nights, according to what you judge necessary based on the strength of the illness and the age and habits of the patient.
And then command him to shave his head with a razor, and then have him sit on a smooth bench, placing his feet upon the stones of the floor.
Then place your palm close to his nose between his eyes. "But when the illness reaches its peak, mark that spot with a tent, and immediately light the olive-oil cautery of which this is the shape:"
"And then gently place that [cautery] on the marked spot so that it descends gradually, allowing your hand to be scorched little by little, and you turn it."
"And then lift your hand gently and look at the spot. If you see that some of the bone has been exposed, about as much as a grain of barley, then lift your hand; but if it does not return to its former state, replace it with another one until you see what I told you."
"And then take a little salt and dissolve it on that spot and let it sit for three days."
"And then place [a bandage] around the wound and leave it until the fire's scar is destroyed." "And then heal the wound with tetrapharmacon until it is healed."
"And they said that when the wound remains open, it's better to tie the arm, as this is more beneficial."
"And some of them said that the tendon should be cauterized within the bone, and hold the cautery there until it burns a part of the bone's surface."
"Then scrape away what has adhered to the bone, then apply treatment."
"And others said that when the cautery reaches the bone, it should press down strongly until the bone cracks; this is the way to perform a small trepanation." "The vapor of the head escapes from this place."
The wound should be kept open for a long time, and then it should be treated until it has healed.
But I have not seen this type of cauterization in all cases, only in some men due to fear; and that one, it seems to me, is better with regard to health when it happens, because the head is uncovered by solution of continuity from it just as we have testified in other members, and even more so when the head of the patient is naturally bald.
And that first type of cauterization is safer and better in my opinion, and with that one and with that operation there is health.
Chapter Two
Of the cauterization of the head.
When there is chronic and long-lasting pain in the whole head and this is prolonged illness, and it is treated with plasters and ointments and poultices and emplasters and warm water and cabbage leaves and linseed poultices and similar remedies, especially if it has already been cauterized once without benefit from any of those causes, then consider whether the patient's head has a strong constitution by nature; if it is not thin or weak in any way, then cauterize it again with another cautery near that first one. And cautionarily cauterize that over the horn of his head with a cautery within which the chain is broken. Break the chain and uncover the bone as much as we have indicated and cauterize it with a cautery in the last part of the head where it is called "the fontanelle" - that is the place where cupping glasses are applied. And soften your hand in this, and do not uncover the bone because the illness suffers great pain different from all other cauteries of the head.
And I say this cautery in its place because it is necessary to cauterize with the same cautery with which you cauterize the horns of the head and the occiput, for that cautery is more subtle than that with which you cauterize the middle part of the head; and this is the form of it.
Chapter Three
Of cautery treatment for non-acute migraine.
When sudden pain occurs in the part of the head or when there is continuous pain inside the brain, let it be evacuated with medicines that cleanse the head and let the other treatment which I have described in previous sections be administered. And if none of this helps, then cautery treatment may be done in two ways: either cautery with an acutely burning medicine, or with iron.
The cautery with a burning medicine is as follows: take a dish of salt and dissolve it in vinegar and then separate the extremities from both ends. And then make an incision with a sharp lancet in the location of the pain on the temple within the area where the chain lies beneath the skin without exposing it.
"Then press upon them with a firm grasp for a descending constriction, and leave them thus for the space of three hours; then untie them and draw them out, and leave the plaster on for two or three days, then pause over the submerged area with a wet cloth until the place becomes warm."
"And then treat that wound until it is healed."
"If you wish, perform some warming medicines which I have written about in the second book of warming remedies."
"However, the cautery with iron is done in this manner; heat the cauterizing iron until it glows red-hot:"
"and is called nail clamps because its head resembles the shape of a nail with some curvature, and in its middle there is a small prominence."
"And then press the cauterizing iron onto the area of pain, keeping your hand steady so that the heat is not scattered, and slowly move the iron back and forth with speed."
"And if the quantity of the cauterization is as great as the size of the wound itself, lift your hand away quickly so that the artery that remains open does not become cauterized, for fear that blood flow ceases."
"And then soak a piece of cotton in saltwater and apply it to the area, leaving it there for three days. Then rinse off with warm water, and then treat the wound until it is healed."
"If you wish, cauterize with the tip of a knife according to cautery, and be careful not to cut an artery in this migraine specifically."
"Chapter Four."
"Regarding the cautery of an ancient headache."
"When you treat a headache with the methods we have mentioned previously and in the divisions of illnesses, but find that it is not effective, then it is necessary to heat the cauterizing knife until it glows red-hot. Then apply your hand to the location of the pain for a moment and press down firmly until you cut through the skin and reach the bone."
"But it is necessary for you to guard against the continuation of the jawbone, which is moved by chewing, and ensure that the nerve is not cauterized or burned, and that there is no spasm."
"And concerning the care and caution regarding blood flow from the artery that is opened, for there is fear in such an opening, especially for one who does not know what they are doing and is untrained or inexperienced. Leave the operation to someone experienced at the beginning."
"And here we come back with a reminder of the method for controlling accidental bleeding from an artery according to its manner in the place where this book is located."
"And if you see this illness not helped by the cautery and that the patient can tolerate it, then cauterize in the middle of the head as we have described, and treat the wound until it is healed."
"And if you wish, administer the cautery that we have mentioned in the chapter on ligation of an artery with cautery using two knives, for this is a better and more certain cauterization."
"Chapter Five."
"Regarding cautery for ear pain."

"And if you apply cautery at several points around the entire ear and it relieves the pain, then treat those locations until they are healed."
"Chapter Six."
"Regarding cautery for strangulation."
"Strangulation that is cured with cautery only refers to those types that come on suddenly due to blood clots, as we have mentioned in the divisions of illnesses."
"Avoid cautery for the type that comes on due to dryness or spasm of a nerve."
"When you treat that type of strangulation with remedies and expectorants but find that your treatment is not effective, then it is necessary to cauterize the patient at three locations: first, at the opening of the ear; second, a little below the temple; and third, at the junction of the two lips."
"And pause your cautery on the side opposite to the affected area, for softening occurs only in the part that appears healed."
"The method of cautery is to cauterize with the cautery instrument on the right side of the ear's tip slightly above the horn of the head, and also on the temple on the other side, the length of which should be according to the length of a finger. Then pause your hand with the cautery until it burns as much as half of its circumference."
"This is the form of the cautery instrument, and it is specifically for use by surgeons. Its form is shown in front of this table." "Nevertheless, even more subtle and precise than this one is the one you see here. It is necessary to use a knife instead of excessive coarseness."

"Chapter Seven."
"Regarding cautery for ancient apoplexy."
"When apoplexy has become chronic and you treat it with the methods we have described but find that your treatment is not effective and the patient does not have a fever, then cauterize them at four locations: on all of their cranial bones; in the middle of the head as we have mentioned; and on the nape of the neck." "The method of cautery is done according to what has been described above."
"And it is also cauterized over the mouth of the stomach; this is the last step. And then treat with what you process."
"Chapter Eight."
"Regarding cautery for fainting caused by blood clots."
"It is necessary to give the patient large diuretics and pills made of blessed thistle at the beginning."
"And then shave the head completely for the patient, and pause on their nape where the plaster of lead is placed, as described in the treatise on plasters."
"And pause there several times, for this is the method of cautery." "Do what you have been told according to that order I have mentioned."
"And then if the patient is healed well with that, do so. But if not, then apply three cauteries on the last part of their head, which should be descending from the highest point of the head down to the lowest point of the neck." "Pause between each cautery for the thickness of a finger, and then treat the cautery with what is processed."
"But if you make an addition and the patient is tolerant of it, then apply cautery in the middle. And if you wish to make an additional addition, then apply cautery on both cranial bones."
"And then treat until healed. And let the cauteries be olive-shaped according to the form shown above."
"Chapter Nine."
"Regarding cautery for paralysis and softening of the entire body."

Afterward, heal him until he is healed, then cauterize with olive oil cautery.
Chapter X.
Of cautery for epilepsy.
The epileptic is not cauterized unless the epilepsy is due to phlegm.
Therefore, first cleanse the brain of him with hellebore at the beginning and also use the other treatment that we have mentioned in the division when the illness is great and does not respond to medicines.
And if it is a child who does not tolerate medicines, then administer purgatives and chew hellebore for him, before that for several days, with proper diet of foods.
And afterward shave the head of the patient, and then cauterize him with one cautery in the middle of the head according to the previous manner, and with another cautery on the back of his head, and cauterize each of his horns. And if the patient is strong and does not respond to medicines, then cauterize him with the cauteries mentioned above, having paralytically softened and mollified his body over the vertebrae of the neck and back.
And let the cautery be olive oil according to what is preceding.
But if the patient is a child, then pause cauterizing with subtle cauteries in this manner:
Chapter XI
Of cautery for melancholy.
When the causes of melancholy are corrupt humidity and thick phlegm, then cauterize him with the cauteries that we have mentioned previously, having paralyzed him.
And if the cause of melancholy is excessive matter that declines to melancholy and the body of the patient is moist, then he should drink hellebore to cleanse his brain according to what has been mentioned in the division.
And afterward shave the head of the patient, and then place a well-prepared plaster made from dove's dung on his head in a circle, and afterward remove it from the middle of the head.
And let the patient be kept quiet and restrained from all parts.
And afterward apply a liniment; and afterwards it is fitting to apply powder of ram's testicles. And heat him over the fire with moderate warmth according to his endurance when placed upon him. And afterward place him on the middle of the head in a circle and let him remain until he is warmed up.
And do this with the patient once each week until he is healed; or if you wish, cauterize him with small points according to his strength, but hold your hand thus with a cautery arranged in this manner: This is the type of cautery that moistens the brain evenly.
And afterward apply powder made from the ashes of wool or flaxseed.
Chapter XII
"For burning off descending water from the head."
"When you have declared the beginning of water in the head by its signs that I mentioned in the division, then begin and give the patient something to drink that cleanses their head, and guard them from all humidity."
"And let them take a bath with warm water for several days."
Approximately command him to raise his head, and cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head; then cauterize it with two cauteries on both temples if it is the beginning of an epilepsy attack with water in both ears, or on one side if it begins only in one ear.
Make incisions with cauteries on all the veins and arteries that are located near the neck; the cauteries should be of such a length as to reach the level of the temples.
And be careful about blood flow when cauterizing; and if you see any blood flowing, remove the cautery with some medicine that you have available.
And bring along knowledge about extraction of arteries and ablation of those, as well as caution regarding blood flow.
Sometimes it is cauterized in the back of the neck near two bones with two cauteries perpendicularly applied.
Capitol 13
Regarding the cauterization of old tears (i.e., treatment of chronic blepharitis or conjunctivitis).
When tears from the eyes are excessive and continuous due to veins and arteries in the visible area of the head being obstructed by a superfluous flow of serous fluid, then cauterize as follows: with the cautery mentioned at the beginning for watery discharge in the ears, and with another cautery in the middle of the head, and with two cauteries on either side of the temples, and with two cauteries on either side of the two bones in the neck. And if you need to add more, then cauterize also on both sides of the cheekbone above the outer corner of the eyebrow with a small cautery.
Capitol 14
Regarding cauterization for foul-smelling breath (i.e., treatment of halitosis).
When you treat someone with the remedy mentioned earlier and it does not prove effective, then prepare and give them to drink the decoction for three nights. Afterwards, shave their head and cauterize it as follows: first with a cautery in the middle and then with an olive-shaped cautery. And also cauterize with a clove-shaped cautery on both eyebrows above the hairline using two cauteries.
And be careful not to burn the artery (i.e., avoid damaging blood vessels).

Capitol 15
Burns for softening eyelids
When the eyelid is softened by illness or humidity, cauterize that eyelid with this lunar cautery; and this is how it is done:
"If you wish, cauterize that area on both eyebrows with two small cauteries, separated from the temples." "And let the length of each cautery be according to the amount of eyebrow present."
"Press the cautery gently onto the skin, so that only a third of the eyelid's thickness is burned."
Chapter XVI
"Regarding cauterization of the eyelids when the hairs inside become inflamed and pierce the eye."
"When the hairs of the eyelid become inflamed and penetrate the eye so that it is pierced, there are two ways to cauterize them: either by burning or using medicinal remedies."
"If cauterizing with fire, command the patient beforehand that they pull out their hairs like those who are plucked for depilation, ensuring they are lengthened and hardened; and if one of them pierces near its origin, then immediately stretch the eyelids using a bandage so that it is not bothered until the hairs have grown back."
"When the hairs have grown back and hardened, then pause the patient's head against a wall, and holding the eyelids open with one hand using a tent-like device made of linen cloth, bring the cautery close to the base of the hairs."
"And hold the cautery wrapped in wax or pitch-plaster over the eyelid."
"And then ignite the cautery of which this is the shape."
"And then cauterize over the figure that you have traced, little by little in many places until the surface of the skin appears like a boxwood leaf."
"A sign that your work is complete is when you see the eyelid of the eye begin to contract and its hairs rise up from the puncture wound."
"And then raise your hand [from the cauterization] and leave it for three days. After that, touch the area gently around the scar until it has healed." "And then dress it with gauze until it is healed."
"But if the hairs return for some reason and the eyelid becomes softened again, then perform the cautery once more just as you had done initially."
"But if the hairs remain on the eyelid and it droops downwards, then cauterize that area until it is raised to its natural position, closed, and the hairs do not obstruct the eye."
"But during cauterization with a burning medicine, you should command the patient to raise their eyebrows until they are fully extended and closed. Then, make a template of boxwood leaf shape from card or cloth."
"And then take some known soap and live coals and mix them well together quickly so that it does not cool down. Then spread this mixture onto the card template shaped like a boxwood leaf, and place it over one or both eyes. The patient should keep their eye covered with a damp cloth while keeping their head still."
"And then place your finger on the medicine and press it gently without moving until the patient feels the bite of the medicine, for the bite is like that of fire."
"And when the patient feels that sensation of being bitten, then lift off the medicine and move it with your fingertip; once the biting sensation ceases, remove the medicine and rinse the eye with water. Then observe whether the eyelid has risen as it should be when lifted by the restriction with fire or incision, for sometimes it is necessary to repeat the operation until the eye is completely closed."
"And then place a border around it so that the edge of the wound is properly sealed. Then dress it with deaffection or another suitable dressing until it heals."
"And you must be very diligent to ensure that no particles of the medicine fall into the eye during your operation. And if the eyelid becomes inflamed after several days and some cause of hairs obstruction occurs in the eye, then repeat your application of the medicine on that area which is inflamed, just as you did at the beginning. And then surround it until it heals."
"And this is the shape of a boxwood leaf template:"
"And know that the eyes of men are diverse in size and smallness, and thus your cauterization should be adjusted accordingly."
"And it shall not be hidden from those who wish to learn this art correctly."
"Chapter XVII."
"Of the cautery of a fistula that is in the tear duct of the eye."
"When you treat a fistula as we have said in the divisions of diseases and your treatment does not improve, then you should cauterize it in this manner: Have the patient hold their head still with their chin on their chest, and have someone else hold the patient's head firmly between their thighs so that it is not moved or shaken by the patient's own head."
"And then place a hot iron in a boxwood leaf template or in pitch on the eye of the patient."
"And then heat the cautery, which has this shape:"
"which is heated, so that it resembles the shape of a reed or a vulture's feather at its tip where the cautery is made."
"If you wish, let it penetrate slightly into the other extremity." "And if you want, let it come out like this:"
"But rather use the one that is heated for better results."
"And then press on the fistula if it is open, and draw out its humor and dry it." "But if it is not open, then open it and extract the pus from it."
"And then take the cautery which is well heated and place it near the eye but not too close to the cautery of the eye at the opening of the nose, so that you do not err with your hand or cause discomfort to the patient. Then place the cautery on the granuloma and burn it."
"And thus if at the beginning of your cautery it enters the bone, that is good. But if not, then repeat the cautery once or twice if necessary, and leave it alone for three days. Then dress it with a dry dressing until it heals."
"But if after eleven days it has not healed, then apply a caustic medicine until the bone is exposed and scrape it according to what you consider necessary in this chapter."
"And there is another method of cautery for fistulas which some first said: Take a narrow knife and cut into the location of the fistula. Then place in that fissure a subtle thread of lead, about one inch long. Then take it up with tweezers while holding it firmly so that the molten lead does not fall into the eye of the patient. And then place this on the eye of the patient covered with a boxwood leaf template or water, for lead adheres to the location of the fistula and heals it wonderfully."
"And if the fistula is healed with what we have said about cautery and treatment, that is good. But if not, then it is necessary to administer a traction of the nose and reduce the fistula into the nasal cavity according to what will be written in its proper place."
"Chapter XVIII."
"Of the cautery of the fissure of the eyelids."
"Fissures occur frequently in the eyelids, and more so in children's eyelids."

"The cauterization of teeth and the softening of vessels."

"Then the patient should rinse their mouth with salt water and hold it for an hour, and then spit it out, because the moist tooth is solidified and the softened gum is strengthened and the corrupted moisture is dried."

"Of cauterization for tooth pain."
"When toothache is caused by coldness, or there is heat or worm in it, and the cure does not work with medicine, then cauterize it in that place in two ways: either cauterize it with a red-hot iron or cauteri with fire."
"To cauterize with a red-hot iron: you take a piece of red-hot iron and place it on the coal fire, then take cotton and wrap it around the tip of the toothpick, and quickly immerse that in boiling water. Then apply that to the painful tooth immediately before it cools."
"And repeat this several times until the power of the heat reaches the root of the teeth."
"Now if you wish, immerse wool or cotton in the aforementioned boiling water, place it on the painful tooth, and then apply that hot iron to the groove of the tooth until the iron reaches its point."
"But however the cauterization with fire is done: you wait until the coal or iron is red-hot, and it should be in the middle of the fire some distance away so that the heat does not reach the mouth of the sick person."
"And then ignite the cautery in whatever shape it may be, place it on the tooth and press it there with your hand until the cautery cools. And do this several times, either that day or the next, until the pain is removed."

"Chapter XXVIII."
"On puncturing an abscess of the liver with a cautery."
When an abscess or boil appears on the liver and you want to know if that boil is in the flesh of the liver or in its covering, then if it is in the flesh of the liver, the patient feels heavy and sharp pain.
And if it is in the covering of the liver, then the pain is very acute and sometimes even discourages physicians from treating it.
Therefore, make the patient turn onto their back; then find the location of the boil with your touch and burn it using an iron that resembles wool, whose shape has been described on the previous page. And cauterize with that cautery until all the chain is burned, until it reaches the nipple, until in ash it remains.
And then treat that one according to how you would treat other boils until it is healed.
And do not use this cautery technique unless you are someone who has studied medicine extensively and has treated many illnesses. Although some people have tried it several times with success, it is still better to leave such operations to those who deserve them and know how to perform them best.
Capítulo XXIX.
Of cautery for pleurisy.
It is said by ancient authors that cautery for pleurisy should be done in this manner: take a dry and hard splinter of aristologist wood (a type of oak), make it into a sharp point as thick as your finger.
And then immerse that splinter in oil and heat it over the fire. Then cauterize with it at a point between the continuation of the furcula and the collarbone; and with two small diagonal cauteries below the jaw on either side, inclining slightly towards the back of the neck; and with two cauteries between the fifth rib and the armpit, inclining slightly backwards; and with another cautery in the middle of the chest; and one on the stomach; and three cauteries on the back; and one cautery between the two shoulder blades; and two cauteries on either side of the chest.
And do not hold the cautery with your hand but place it in a suitable holder for the purpose of cauterizing.
And they also said that some people use a cautery made of iron like a rod and heated it until it glows red-hot, placing it between the shoulder blades until it reaches the boil and makes it suppurate in order to heal it, as we have mentioned for the boil on the liver.
But in this operation with cautery there is danger, because many people die immediately if it happens in the location of a fistula that does not heal.

When you treat the illness of the spleen with what we have said about division and curing, if your treatment does not succeed, then cauterize it in one of three acceptable ways:
"The thickness between each cautery should be that of a finger or slightly more, and the manner of the cautery should be as we have described for the burning of the liver equally."
"Do not press your hand on the cauteries; let the patient lie on their side."
"And another method of cautery is that you should light the cauteries having two vents, which we have mentioned in the chapter on dislocation of the muscle, and raise the collarbone which is situated towards the left side where the patient's chest lies."
"And your elevation should be such that you raise the collarbone along the length of the body, so that cauteries may be applied according to the width of the chest."
"And then introduce both vents promptly and simultaneously into that opening where the collarbone enters from the other side."
"Then extract the cautery, making four cauteries in total."
"If you wish, cauterize with a cautery having three vents."
"Then remove the cautery because it has been applied for too many days, since this is more harmful than all other causes preceding in treatment."

Chapter XXXV
Concerning cautery after excision of tumors.
When you cut arteries, then ignite the cautery that resembles a ray. Apply the cauterizing iron to the cut artery and push it in until it reaches the opening of the vein from which blood flows.
And do this once or twice, because if there are too many [arteries], then cauterize each one individually according to what has been said earlier, and then dress those wounds until they are healed.
And if you use a large cautery on some [arteries], it is more likely to cause injury.
Chapter XXXVI
Of the cauterization of fistulas made by nature in the region around the anus.
If nature does not obey the operation recommended in its place and the patient becomes frightened by this, then it should be treated with cautery.
Therefore, when there is some fistulous opening in the anus region and it is old, corrupted by bad humor and moisture, then it is more appropriate to probe that with a subtle tent and thus determine the quantity of its depth using the aforementioned tent.
Then apply the cautery that resembles a ray. And then insert that cautery while lit into this fistula, according to how deep the tent entered it.
And repeat applying the cautery until those corrupted tissues are completely burned once or twice or three times, according to how much it is necessary for you.
And be careful not to burn nerves and veins; or if the fistula goes into the body of the bladder or the body of the gut, then be careful about all those places. And do not do this unless the fistula is in a fleshy location and you think that it is not penetrating.
"And next, heal the wound within until it is healed."
"And if the wound is sealed and its matter has been removed, and no new signs appear for some time, then you will know that it has entirely healed."
"And if the matter was not all removed from it, then you will know that it is penetrating or spreading into the surrounding flesh, or corrupted due to one of those causes which we will mention later."

Chapter XLII
Of cauterizing pain in the teeth.
Some pains in the back arise from several causes, such as from cold or from a blow or from excessive evacuation, and from similar causes due to the infusion of moist and warm matter.
Cautery is only used in this one condition which occurs due to an effusion of hot matter.
Therefore, it is necessary to evacuate the pain with pills made of dried figs and similar ones. These should be placed on the back where there is pain in three lines according to its length, because as many cauteries as there are fingers should be done in each line, depending on the amount of tolerance for the pain and the strength of it.
And cauterization should be done with a pointed cautery.
And if you wish, cauterize with three or four cauteries using a triangular cautery in this manner:
De ventositat de la cossella e de lo cor.
Cauterization at the beginning of swelling.
Many times this disease is seen in young children, and one of the signs that indicates its beginning in children is when it occurs above their anus, accompanied by swelling and redness.
And if it appears on the last vertebra of the back or neck, and begins to rise above the other vertebrae
And therefore, when you see this [swelling], in order to prevent it from spreading further, cauterize around it with a hot iron that is circular in shape so that the cautery covers the entire inflamed part of the spine.
And if you wish, cauterize around the spine with a cautery rod two or three lines thick So that the points are close together.
And afterwards, treat the affected area with what has been said [about cauterization].
But be sure not to administer cautery to swelling that is caused by spasm of nerves.
Chapter XLIV
Of the cauterization of podagra and pain in joints.
When pain in the joints is caused by cold humors that have infiltrated each member of the body, thus when pains occur in the feet, it is customary for doctors to call this gout properly.
And therefore, since gout caused by cold humors cannot be cured and the pains are not removed, cautery is resorted to.
You cauterize that area appropriately with several cauteries in the circuit of the ankle joint using moxa cauteries.
And this is the form of olive oil cautery:
If it is necessary to make points on the surface of the foot, then do so with a pointed cautery. If pains push inside the joints or other connections, and this happens often, then cauterize each painful point with three cauteries or four in total using this olive-oil cautery.
If more cauteries are needed than these, then cauterie each one and do not press too hard with the cautery beforehand, only enough to heat the surface of the skin.
If pains push inside both knees, then administer the cautery that we have described in this chapter.
If pains are only on one side and are called cramps, then prick around the belly button in this way: prick along these two lines.
If pains remain on them, then prick around the belly button above each knot and also on the palm of the hand.
If pains push near the joints or muscles of the thighs, then cauterize each of all the helpers (i.e., the points around the knee).
Do not rely solely on this cautery to treat illness without good regimen and receipt of medicines, for if a good regimen is followed and the disease is evacuated with this cautery alone, God willing, it will heal.
Chapter XLVI
**On Cauterization of Fractures**
When rupture occurs in the groin and intestines protrude before the hernia, and this is how it begins, then you should order the patient to abstain from his usual diet on that day and to use gentle laxatives so that the protruding opening of his belly may subside.
Then approach the patient between your thighs and press firmly on their buttocks, commanding them to hold their breath until the tumor or protruding intestines appear clearly.
Then return with your hands and press the rupture onto the bone of the pelvis, indicating the extremity that is like half a circle among the upper ribs.
And then apply the cautery in this form:
until it becomes very red and sparks are emitted. Then reduce the intestines or tumor to its natural position, and press your hand firmly over the spot so that the intestines do not escape.
And you have now put your hands between the patient's thighs and allowed him to rest on one elbow, and others support his shoulders and hips, while he holds his own buttocks with his hands.
Then place the cautery on that spot with your left hand holding the cautery, and press firmly until you reach the bone of the pelvis in the middle of the thigh; repeat this procedure with another burn if you have not succeeded in the first attempt.
Be sure to observe carefully the condition of the intestines during your cautery, and do not burn them accidentally lest the patient suffer death or some other great harm.
Know that if you do not reach the bone with the cautery, you have not completed your operation.
And it is necessary that the cautery used for children be small according to their size and proportion.
Then cleanse the spot of the cautery for three days with warm water so that the scar may heal properly.
Then dress him with other remedies until he is healed.
And the patient should lie on his back for forty days so that the wound may be sealed and healed properly.
It is necessary that the patient's diet be regulated according to the length of time for his healing, so that his belly may not be disturbed by excessive effort during recovery and intestines do not protrude.
And when he wants to get up after forty days, gradually administer solid food, and keep him in bed for another forty days.
And he should refrain from work, overindulgence in food and drink, and violent coughing or vomiting, for if he uses this regimen of healing entirely as prescribed, he will be completely healed.

"Chapter one."
"Of the cure of water which is contained in the head of children."
You know that this disease often happens in children during childbirth, and when the midwife pulls out the head of the child without its life.

But you did not see this disease except in children; and of all those whom you saw, they were taken by death, and thus it seems to me that it is a very sad sight.
And you have seen that the head of one child was so full of water that it could not cease to swell because of the greatness of its head, and more that the moisture increased until it died.
And this moisture is either gathered between the neck and the skull, or it is gathered behind the skull on top of the brain.
For the operation in that case if the moisture is between the neck and the skull and the abscess is small, it is necessary for you to make an incision in the middle of the head with a scalpel in the width, and let its length be two fingers so that the moisture may flow out, and this is the form of the opening:

Chapter Two
When there occurs some cataract or wound in the eye or foot, and it becomes chronic and does not respond to medication treatment, then the last resort is the incision of those two arteries.

Chapter III
Of the Extraction of Arteries Which Are in the Temples
When there occurs an ancient migraine or sharp headache due to moist humors and bad qualities in the temples' nerves, or if it is ancient and vehement and seems like these, and it is treated with medicinal remedies but does not improve, then we have found useful in such diseases extraction of the arteries in the temples, or cauterization of those as we have shown.
And the manner of the operation and extraction of these is that the patient shaves the hair which is on the temples; and understand where the artery appears in the temple, for it will be shown to you by its pulsation, and is hidden unless in a few men due to great friction.
And if it is hidden from you, then let the patient's head be strengthened with some cloth; and also rub the place with a rough cloth or evaporate the place with hot water until the artery appears manifestly to you. And then take the scalpel of which this is the shape: And then carefully cut along the artery with it until you reach the artery; and also cut onto it and extract that from above until you extract it from the artery and discover those tunics which are beside it, on all sides.
But if the artery is small, then wrap that end of the needle around it, and cut a lengthwise slit in it according to how much its ends are stretched out and contracted, and there will be no flow of blood, for otherwise it would not rise up at all.
And then evacuate three or four veins' worth of blood from it.
And if the artery is large, then you should tie it in two places with strong double thread of cotton or long strings, so that it does not suffer putrefaction before the wound is consolidated and there is no flow of blood.
And then incise the excess which is between two ligatures; and do this at that time or some other time.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a cautery at the bone or with a needle cautery until its ends are incised, and this will be in place of that operation.
And it is better to do this if the patient has a fever or is of warm complexion; for cautery is among those causes which help fine humors, and therefore it is better in such cases.
And also after the extraction of the arteries, you should cover the place with cotton wool, well-powdered; and then it should be cured with dry medications which cause flesh to grow, and with bandages until it is healed.
And if during the operation there occurs a flow of blood from the artery again, then stop that blood either with a cautery or by covering the place with cotton wool and pressing your hand until the bleeding stops. And if none of those causes is present to you, then press your finger on the place until the bleeding stops, and bore the place with water of great coldness until the sharpness subsides, and strengthen it as is necessary.
And that which is easiest in extraction of an artery is to cauterize the vein with this cautery having two points, for after you have indicated both places with a tent, and then ignited each one separately, they will reach the bone and incise the vein in two places, so that what is between its ends may be elongated, for it will not be completely consolidated by this cautery.
And this is the shape of the cautery:
And let the sharpness of both points be equal. But if they are not too sharp then two points are better; for if they are very sharp they come together closely with each other, and thus do not incise the flesh quickly.
Therefore because there is some thickness in them, the heat of the fire is retained within them and the flesh is incised gently.
This work is best among all works and very light.
Between two ribs, the space should be as thick as a finger.

"Chapter VII."
"On the treatment of earache."
Some children sometimes exit from the wombs of their mothers unperforated, and this has happened to some men, deafness in the ear due to plague or meat not cooked well at that time; and this deafness sometimes occurs in the front of the Eustachian tube of the ear which does not consent hearing, and sometimes it is in the parts outside.
This one that happens at the front of him, which does not comprehend hearing, is in the most serious conditions for difficult recovery.
And in that one where it succeeds to hear, you should put your ear near the patient's mouth and listen carefully. And then if there is obvious deafness, open that with a probe of subtle spatula whose shape it has.
If the tip of it where it should be inserted is small, one part being sharp and the other blunt, then the probe should be doubled on two sides so that it does not harm the ear.
And then if the deafness is due to flesh grown in the ear or in its membrane, immediately use those with fine forceps and cut that violently obstructing part inside where all the flesh has remained.
And if deafness is at the front of the ear, then take a fine wire and heat it gently on fire, and carefully put it in the membrane of the ear.
And then if you have felt great deafness with what the patient feels easily, due to heaviness of hearing, relieve that pressure gently without hurting the nerves of the ear.
And then put a bandage around the ear according to its size of circumference that you have wrapped in Egyptian ointment; and do this for some days until you are sure of the consolidation of the wound. Or else take wax and pour it into boiling water, grind it thoroughly and use it, and if sometimes the ear is already inflamed then you should remove that wax and replace it with other wax that you have prepared with rose oil until the inflammation ceases; and then cure it until it heals.
But if bleeding occurs, then soak a sponge or cloth in cold water and apply it to the ear, and use other treatment that stops the flow of blood, which is mentioned in the treatise on distillation.
Chapter VIII
Of the cure for inflammation that occurs in the eyelids.
"Dry out the moisture that occasionally appears on the eyelids of the eyes; sometimes they are dry."
Therefore you must have those tweezers or pincers ready, and quickly spread the edges of the wound apart, and then carefully pause over the locations described.

Chapter IX
Treatment for swelling that comes to the eyelids of the eyes
Sometimes it happens that in the eyelids of the eyes there appears some cause which resembles swelling in its hardness and toughness, and for this reason it is called swelling.
And it is an accumulation of thick moisture in the lower eyelid above.
Therefore, their operation is to examine whether the size of it appears from beneath the eyelid and is enlarged in all parts. The cause of this is lightness.
Therefore make an incision over that with a scalpel for the width, and promptly examine it throughout its extent with ease so that it may be evacuated, then suspend it with hooks and cut it.
And if the wound is large, then adjust it with a suture; and care for it until it has healed.
But if it is small, do not worry about that because the ointment of it restores it and covers it over.
And if the size is decreasing within the partitions inside the eyelid, then you must evacuate the eyelid and suspend the size with hooks, another one that you will need for incision, and press it throughout its extent, and if the eyelid through incision has extended, do not worry about anything.
And moreover concerning the incision, wash the wound with salt water; and treat the place with something that covers it within until it has healed.
Chapter X
Concerning the treatment of a boil on the upper eyelid.
A boil is a growth that arises in the tissues of the upper eyelid, and it commonly occurs in children. It aggravates wounds of those who suffer from them and affects their sight much; they cannot look at the brightness of the sun because tears flow from their eyes.
"You therefore have them lie on their backs all the time, and even during sleep; and if you see moisture on their eyelids, which have already been covered by swelling, then encroachment of inflammation is apparent in the eye. And when you press that area with your fingers, you feel pulsation within it."
"There is a certain way of operating which the patient places their head on the pillow and props up drapes on both sides, and makes a circle according to the quantity that contains earthenware of all parts. Then they place that over that and press with their fingers all around so that arcernac may be aggregated in the middle of the circle. And then they finish by striking in the middle of that humidity with spatula incidentally through the latitude, and the fissure should not be greater than the fissure made in trepanation."
"But first of all you should cut the entire chain until you reach arcernac, for in most cases it proceeds through a fissure according to the form of grayish-white matter."
"Then remove that with drapes which you have wrapped between your fingers to the parts outside and roll it between your right hand and left hand until it is gone."
"And be sure not to adjust in the fissure lest with the spatula you reach the humor and damage that."
"But if your arcernac does not appear at the beginning of the fissure, then adjust in the fissure a little with ease until arcernac proceeds. And then remove it as we have said. And then soak drapes in water and wine and apply them on that place and wipe those with powders."
"And so some take salt and sprinkle it on the concavity of the fissure to prevent that which remains of that humor."
"And then dress that until it is healed."

Chapter XI
Of methods for touching the humor.
When the eyelids of the eyes are opened by adjusting the natural lashes and they are hardened, then those touch the eye and cause ways of illnesses such as constant tears and softening of eyelids and tissue which might be caused by destruction of the eye that is called tactinim of the eye, and it is cured in four methods: either by cautery with fire; or with sharp medicine according to what has been said earlier in the chapter on cautery; or it is done by incision and suturing; or by cannulae according to what I will say.
And therefore you should first examine the head of the illness in its place; also turn the eyelid of the eye with your left hand, and if it is turned, well then. And if not, insert a needle into that where there is flesh thicker than the eyelid, and make the needle penetrate through that flesh above, and close to — but not on — the edge of the eyelid where the lashes grow, and pull the thread upwards with the eyelid, and turn it with the ray.
And then make an incision in the thicker part of the eyelid below the adjusted lashes with a spatula incidentally opening from the larger lacrimal to the smaller lacrimal; and then remove the thread and press gently on the eyelid with small powders of cotton or cloth, and then sew over the eyelid with silk, and it should be like the figure of a bay leaf.
But nevertheless the figure should be according to the amount of elevation of the eyelid, because this is varied among people, for some need that a great quantity of the eyelid should be incised according to the amount by which the eyelid is softened.
And then make an incision with the spatula over the two lines that you have sewn and begin from the larger lacrimal towards the smaller lacrimal. And if your fissure is close to the natural lashes, as thick as a king's ring.
And then place the onion in one of the two angles of the corner of the eye and then excoriate that entirely and then adjust the two lips with silk or thin wool thread, and wipe away the blood, and make what is superfluous of the threads adhere to both eyebrows with some viscid causes, if you wish to do so, otherwise do not bother.
And then leave the stitches alone for three or four days, and then dress it.
And if you wish, leave the plaster without stitching and dress it with something that dries and constrains, for the eyelid is raised by the sealing of the plaster and the adhesion of the lute; but stitching is better. And therefore this is what the ancients said about tactinim.
But nevertheless if there is anxiety about the illness and it has a good outcome, then fear should not be in him.
And there is another method of tactinim, which is to sew on the eyelid in the shape of a bay leaf and then lift up the eyelid with three onions separated and gathered together in this way:
But if that is not possible, then hold the onions and whatever is not swallowed. Then take an needle with thread and put it in the middle of the figure and insert the other thread near the larger lacrimal, and the second one near the smaller lacrimal, and adjust the threads between your fingers evenly, and then with those lift up your hand gently and elevate the entire corner on which is sewn as we have recommended.
And then adjust the two lips of the plaster with stitching and dress it when it has healed.
But if an abscess occurs over your incision or fissure, cut that abscess with a cautery and its surroundings, and dress it with egg.
And sometimes it happens that the eyelid itself turns over its lashes. Therefore you should use what has been said about incision in that case; but stitching is more artificial.
Tactinim with cannulae is done in this way: And it is that you turn the eyelid and cut the figure underneath as has been said; and then by art make two cannulae or two thin rods, of which the length should be according to the length of the eyelid and the breadth of them should be less than the breadth of the spatula. And you have already incised at both ends where you hold the threads
Then attach each one with subtlety above what is superfluous of the eyelid of wool, and press the cannulae at both ends with great force, and leave them like that for a few days, because the corner will die and become necrotic and will fall in as you put it.
But if it is delayed, then break it with tweezers, and dress the plaster when it has healed
Therefore when it is solidified, the eyelid is elevated and the lashes do not prick.
And this is the shape of two cannulae which you will make like this: And it should have the quantity of length and breadth neither more nor less.

"Chapter XIII."
"On the treatment of eachcretation that occurs in the eyelid."
When there is swelling in the eye socket and contraction of the eyelid when it is constricted either on the right or left side due to poor consolidation of plague or section or cautery, this is called leporina. This herniation can be natural or accidental, caused by consolidation of plague or section or cautery, or similar to those afflicted with evil.
The manner of performing the operation is as follows: first, you should remove that consolidation and separate the eyelids. Then insert a wick of cloth into the space between them and bind it tightly so that they may heal.
You should not use remedies for the eye which cause dryness and constriction. For if you do so, it will return to its previous state of herniation and never be truly healed.
Use remedies which soften the eye, such as fenugreek and the decoction of rose leaves. Also use eyewash made with water in which alta has been cooked and strained, or diacilicon, which is softened with a little oil. Immerse the wick in these preparations and cure with them.
And the treasure of your cure will be to persist diligently without allowing any consolidation to form, following the method that was used at the beginning.
This operation that we have described is nothing more than a correction of herniation which does not allow the eyelids to return to their previous state.
Chapter XIV
Of the cure for herniation that occurs in the left eyelid
This herniation which occurs in the left eyelid is called true herniation and it can be either congenital or acquired.
Acquired herniation occurs due to injury, cut, burn, or other similar causes.
The procedure for operating on acquired herniation is to take a needle with a double thread and insert it into the flesh. Then pass it from the left lacrimal duct to the right one, ensuring that both ends of the thread are in the flesh.
"And then stretch the skin over the parts above with forceps, hold it with forceps, and cut it with a broad scalpel." "And this figure is called the Greek letter lambda."
"And then take some skin from that side of the acute angle on the left side of it, as much as necessary to close the eyelid."
"And then join together the separated parts and sew them with wool thread; and then dress it with whatever we have said about softening medicines and bandages until it is healed."
"And if there should be any discharge through the incision or suture or cautery, it is necessary that you make a flat incision over the skin of the eyelid as described above." "And then separate with gauze between two lips as we have said."

"Chapter XXIV."
"Of the treatment of flesh torn in the nose."
When flesh of diverse colors grows in the nose, which some say is due to a heavy scorpion or a large polyp, and this illness is called polyps because it resembles that fish. And its flesh is cancerous, ulcerated, dark-colored; and the flesh of the lion is not of dark color.
Therefore, in that which is not fraudulent nor cancerous lion flesh, you should place the sick person's hands between your breasts and put them in the sun, and cover their nose and ears with that flesh. And properly extract that from the external parts, and properly cut with a sharp, subtle knife what you have grasped of those parts when you know that all the flesh is removed; for if anything remains there due to some cause which makes excision possible, then quickly remove it with one of the subtle instruments with ease where nothing remains. But if there is an ulcer with sanguineous matter or an aposteme that is cauterized, then you should do what is necessary; but if it is of malignant ulcers, then you should cut and cauterize until the sanguineous matter is removed and all the cavities are opened.
And properly apply to the wound a dressing with wine and honey or wine. But if the wound is open and there proceeds from it humidity towards the palate, then you should know that it has already healed.
But if the humidity does not penetrate as it should, then you know that within it there is flesh still adhering to the overlying bones of the nostrils where excision has not reached.
Therefore, you should insert a thread of sufficient thickness into it and twine it with numerous knots, leaving between each knot the quantity of a finger or less, and then apply it to the patient's nostril with a hook or any other means which is possible for him.
And after this is done, have him inhale the odor of something pleasant until it reaches the canals of the nostrils, and let him blow his nose into his garment, for many people commonly do this action in schools, and it will be easier for him who wishes to do so.
And properly tie the two extremities of the thread, one extremity which comes out over the nose and the other which remains inside the nose, and apply a suture of flesh with the knots that are on the thread, and do this until you know that the flesh is attached to the knots of the thread.
And then remove the thread and apply to the nose a bandage with honey, and do this for three days or more until the dressing has eaten away all that is left of the flesh.
And finally, apply to the nose a lead cannula for several days until it is healed.
But if you wish for a drying cure, use that one.

On the holes made in the outer parts of the nose.
Frequently it is necessary to make holes in the outer parts of the nose, and it is greatly praised and commended in those days when much care is taken for its regard.
And therefore you should insert that at the beginning of the appearance of that, and remove all of it. And afterwards apply a poultice or cautery or burning medicine to the place where it is located.
But if its incision is delayed until it becomes magnified, then consider whether it is blackish, hard, of dark color and of little sensation; then do not touch it with iron because it is a malignant abscess. For often that which you cut into this abscess will bring great mischief to him upon whom it was.
And if the abscess is of light touch and not of dark color, and sometimes incision is possible in all of it, then evacuate the patient and cut into it without fear, and cure the place with that which dries and constrains until it is healed.
Here is Chapter XXVI:
On the suture of the nose, lips, and ear when continuity is dissolved by wound or ulcer.
Know that when you encounter dissolution of continuity in one of these cartilage, pay little attention to this operation unless you are among certain men.
Therefore, when you find some cause of these, such as a fresh wound with its blood, then join the two lips of the wound with suture and apply cure until it is healed.
And if the lips of dissolved continuity are already separated and each is quite healthy, then you must excise each part from its visible root until there comes forth no blood. And then join the two lips with suture and strengthen them, and over them sprinkle dragon's blood powder and some rue powder, and apply a piece of diaphoretic plaster or some other consolidating plaster upon the powder, and let it lie for two or three days. Then remove it, change the plaster, and allow the wounds to close by themselves. Afterwards cure with ointment until they are healed.
But the manner of suture is that you should join the dissolution of continuity with needles as we have described in the suture of the belly, or join it with a thread as you know how to do.
Here is Chapter XXVII:
Of extraction of those causes that appear in the two lips inside
Often seen in some men inside the parts of their lips certain hard tumors resembling grains of barley, and some are smaller and others larger.
Therefore you should turn back the lips and split each of those hardnesses on all sides, and afterwards perform the excision by filling up the place with saffron thoroughly ground until it is tightened, and then wash it with vinegar and dress the wound with astringent water, until it is healed.
Capítulo XXVIII
Of the incision of attached flesh in the gums
Often when there is a swelling above the attached gum which the ancients called albus, then you should suspend that with a ligature or hold it with forceps and incise it along its length, and let the sanies flow out and the blood.
And afterwards apply saffron thoroughly ground or other astringent or excitative powders.
And if that flesh returns again, incise it once more and cauterize it, because then it will not return.

"Chapter XXX."
"Of tooth extraction."
"So that you cure the tooth of its pain with all care and diligence in every extraction of it, for there is no cause that should hinder the view of it when it is extracted, for it is a sublime substance that is exposed during the extraction of it."
"But when it is necessary to extract that tooth, firmly you must be certain what the painful tooth is, for many times the pain deceives and seems to be in a healthy tooth, and you extract that one but the pain does not cease until the diseased and painful tooth is extracted, for we have already seen this through the operation of barbers."
"So when you are certain what the painful tooth is and know it well, then it is necessary that you scarify around the tooth with a spatula in which there is some virtue, until it is dissolved from the gum of the entire socket, and then slowly with your tweezers or with subtle forceps little by little until that concavity is reached."
"And then apply strong forceps for good grasp or sturdy pressure and place the head of the diseased tooth between your fingers that you hold it well so that it does not move, and pull the tooth straight in such a way that you do not break it, nor cause greater pain to the sick."
"And therefore if it is not forgotten, then take one of those instruments and place it on that side of all the parts with ease and effort, causing the removal of it as was done at the beginning."
"But if the tooth is loose or decayed, then it is necessary that you fill that cavity with cloth and strengthen it with the end of a thin needle so that it is not broken at the time of your traction on it with forceps."
"And it is necessary that you work carefully with scarification around the entire gum and guard well your force so that you do not break it and leave a part of it behind, and return on the diseased one for the malice that may be greater than the first pain of it."
"And beware that you do not do what many ignorant and arrogant barbers do in their haste and boldness over the extraction of teeth, without administering what we have recommended; for many times they bring upon men great diseases which are more difficult to treat than if they had been broken, and the roots of them completely removed or some of those, or that they extract it with some bones of the jaw as we have often witnessed."
"And after the extraction of it, let the mouth be washed with warm wine or vinegar mixed with salt."
"And if there is flow of blood from it, as often happens, then sprinkle some quantity of sage and pour it on the wound, and if that sage does not suffice, cauterize."
"The shape of the strong and subtle forceps with which the tooth is grasped at the beginning should be of long extremity, short handle, and thick, so that they do not bend when you use them."
"And let them be such that when stretched with those, they are not bent, being short at the ends."
"And let them be made of India steel, well-made with straight extremities, and at the ends of those have teeth which fit into each other and stretch by compression descent forcefully, and sometimes the extremities of those are like the shape of a bird's beak called 'ciconia', that is to say, they are strong in constriction."
