And when you see that the thread is cutting into the flesh, then cut away that until those flesh are incised which are between the two ends of the thread and release them."
"And also cure the wound until it is consolidated and healed."
"But if the opening of the fistula is not penetrating, then cause it to penetrate in whatever way you are prepared, but do not penetrate too far into the anus lest you injure the lappet. And also heal it with that until it is healed."
"The form of the spatula with spines with which the fistula is opened: its curvature is very sharp and the other end is not sharp, for it should not injure anything that is not necessary, as sometimes happens."
"Regarding the suture of hemorrhoids in which blood flows from the incision of those."
"Hemorrhoids are of two kinds: for some are between the buttocks resembling coal, or those which are with thick fat." "And of these some are small and large, and blood flows from them constantly and they are many, and they are outside the anus, and at the extremities of it."
"But those hemorrhoids that are outside the anus are according to few humidity, from which flows yellow water or a little blood with continuous flow, and they are according to the color of the body."
"And the cure for those that are inside the anus is to tell the patient to squat down and express until the anus opens up and the hemorrhoids appear." "Then you grasp them with forceps or hold them with your fingernail and also cut those off at their roots."
"But if the forceps are not held in those because of the humidity and softness of them, then grasp them with rough cloth or with cotton or with a wool bandage, extract those with your said fingers, and also cut those off and sprinkle some powdered myrrh on that wound to cauterize those instead; or cauterize those according to what is said in the chapter on cauterizing." "And then cure them with another cure until they are healed."
"And if the anus does not obey evacuation, then cauterize the patient with a small biting cautery, and wash it with water that is in the anus so that it may easily evacuate when the patient strains."
"But if the hemorrhoids are outside the anus, then their cause is light and it is that you grasp them with your fingernail or with forceps, cut those off, and cure them with what we have said until they are healed."
"And he who makes an incision should administer sutures to them in this manner: take thread wrapped around and put it into a needle and also extract the hemorrhoids from their roots above, and make the thread penetrate with the needle into the root of the other side, and wrap both ends of the thread on one side further away from the needle, and stretch the hemorrhoid strongly with tension, and then tie the thread and remove the needle."
"And do this for all, except one, which you should not touch, so that the excess blood flows from that."
"And first place a cloth dipped in rosemary oil or cerote on the back, and then place it on his buttocks, commanding him to remain still." "And then put that cloth inside a box, and when it is dried out, use those or another cure until he/she is healed."
"And when these are old and not useful in that medicine, then you should remove them with the point of a spatula or your nail until they are moistened, and wipe off the pus that is on top, which prevents consolidation. Then cure them until they are consolidated."
"Now you should remove that very violent pus, first making sure not to wound and to scrape until they are healed."
"Know that hemorrhoids are like nails, and they are not red but of the color of the skin resembling a small nail head, and they are found throughout the body but more commonly in the parts below the hips and buttocks, and there pain is felt."
"And then that which is in the circuit of the nail should be incised and held with forceps or a hook, and eradicated from its root. Then cure it."
"And if you wish, cauterize the second one that is mentioned in its chapter."
"But a verruca is a small, rough, fistulous elevation on the surface of the body, and many times they appear in the majority of children."
"And its cure is that you stretch and incise it, or remove it with a file or with tweezers until it is completely removed." "And if you wish, cauterize it with fire or with an acute medicine, as we have said."
"But a formica is a small, fatty or fistulous elevation on the surface of the body, commonly found on the forehead, and when it is touched it feels like the bite of an ant, and it appears around the body."
"And its cure is that you take a feather quill of a dove or an eagle feather, and it should be strong, and place it over the ant so that the circle of the quill contains it in its parts; then rotate your hand with the quill so as to incise what is around the ant, and press it on the flesh, and eradicate the ant from its root."
"And if you wish, do this with a wax or iron cautery according to this method:"
"Let the end of the cautery be drawn out to a fine point, smooth (i.e. not perforated), wrapped in such a way that when it is placed on the said part, its application and rotation are facilitated."
"And if you wish to cauterize the ant according to what has been said before in the chapter of cautery regarding the cauterization of warts."
"Regarding the shape of the instruments used for giving circumcision:"
"Sometimes circumcision is performed with silver or brass or melted wax or dough. And sometimes it is done with those instruments that are small and large according to the size of those for which they are used, because the one used in the cure of young children should be small; and those whose glans are short or retractile, their circumcisions should be small."
"The form of a large and rare circumcision:"
"The head of the catheter at its upper end, where the foreskin is tied, should be wide in this manner; and it should be distinguished from where the foreskin is tied like this."
"And the lower extremity of it, which is inserted into the glans, should be smooth (i.e. not open) and round, tapering to a fine point; and on one side of it there are two slits, and on the other side one slit, and the width of the slit should be according to the thickness of the glans and foreskin."
"And let the bag in which the medicines of bladder or wool are placed be about a span long."
"And it should be pierced with many slits so that as it rotates, the space between the slits is equal to the thickness of the finger."
"And then insert into those slits a strong thread of raw flax, or envelope them with it, and attach the bag to it. And when the bag is placed on it, tighten it at the head of the catheter between two distinctions with a strong constriction, and then cauterize with that each slit: But the manner of cauterizing with it is that you fill the bag with warm medicine, according to the amount of tolerance of the patient; and take care that it is not too hot nor too cold."
"And then the patient should lie on his back, raise his hips up, and place underneath him either cloth or linen. And then anoint the extremity of the cautery with oil or albumen of egg or mucilage of fenugreek or similar substances; then insert it into the glans easily, and then the assistant takes the bag and squeezes it with both hands and presses it violently until everything that is in the catheter is evacuated into the bladder. And then remove the catheter and the patient raises his hips to the wall."
"And if the medicine forces him to urinate, let him hold back as much as he can. And if he sleeps with it all night long, it is more likely to be helpful."
"But if you circumcise him in the case of diarrhea and frequent urination, then repeat the cautery until he is healed."
"And if there are hemorrhoids or painful abscesses in the glans, then use a small and round catheter like those with which children are circumcised."
"Regarding the treatment of plagues:"
"I have already said in the division of the treatment of plagues what was appropriate for this book, and I say now the treatment of those with maimed limbs using medicine according to their condition."
"Therefore I say that plagues are differentiated according to how they are caused and according to the place where they occur."
"And the causes by which plagues often occur are such as penetration of a dart or incision with a scalpel or knife or blow with a lance or club or arrow or similar causes."
"But the plagues that occur in the limbs of the body are such as plague that falls on the head, neck, foot, belly, thigh or similar ones on the members."
"And I am recounting the treatment of some plagues because this reasoning and rule applies to other plagues as well."
"And I will begin with simple head wounds, for the memory of compound ones will come at the beginning of the third chapter."
"Therefore I say that when a simple wound occurs on the head and it is not a fracture, then consider: if it is due to penetration by a dart or something similar, and only the scalp has been injured and it is a large wound, you fear that an abscess may form as a result of this illness. Then quickly perform cephalic phlebotomy and do not delay in doing so; and remove from him as much blood as the force of his strength allows, if no other cause prohibits phlebotomy, especially if the blood that comes out of the wound is little."
"The lobotomy is the opposite of the plague's departure, keep the patient from drinking water or eating heavy foods."
"If you prohibit any cause during the miniaturization process, it would be reduced in size and not approach meat nor wine."
"And pause over the plague, if it happens to cause an aposteme (abscess), it is customary to cut it open and dress it with oil mixed with roasted rose or wine in which it becomes thicker/stoptical."
"But if you are sure of an abscess having formed, then pause over the plague (abscess), if it is fresh and its blood has not yet changed from that which caused it."
"And if the air has already changed from that, then pause over the inflamed ulcer until it is healed."
"But if the plague is large or caused by an incision of space and similar ones, and its lips are not properly closed due to dust, then adjust them with a suture according to what I will recommend for the stitching of the lips."
"But if the plague is already uncovered by bone and skin, and it is not retention of it but only a small suspension, then incise it and treat the plague with medicines and restore the flesh in place of sinew. Command the patient to be fed foods that are coarse: heads of cabbage and similar ones, until he/she is restored."
"And if you find putrefaction in the sinew and it does not adhere to the bone, then incise all that is corrupt and remove it. Then treat it."
"But if in these plagues there is an artery or vein from which a flow of blood can come about, and it is not cut off by medicines, then inquire about the artery. If you find that it has not been incised, then incise it with a scalpel, or tie it. And if necessity prompts that what has been said does not work, then cauterize until the blood flow is stopped."
"And if these plagues are simple and small, then their cause is light. Therefore, powder them with this powder, so that it may solidify before the air changes them."
"And the manner of making the powder is as follows: Take one part oil of sesame, two parts dragon's blood resin, three parts myrrh resin; all well-ground and sifted. With this powder fill the ulcer, and bind it with a good binding so that it may adhere to it and coagulate with blood."
"And properly bind that above with powder and leave it alone. Do not remove it while it is still moist, so that the medicine does not lose its efficacy before it is healed."
"And sometimes one may use cauterization alone when oil of sesame and dragon's blood resin are not available."
"And this same procedure is done in large ulcers when it is convenient to pause upon them and bind them tightly, especially fresh ulcers with their blood, which the air has not yet altered."
"And if the air has altered them, and especially when it is summer time, then it is not suitable to apply this powder. In that case treat those ulcers with what has been said: pause upon them with some ointments made from digestives, or pause upon them with flour paste made with water and honey until they become dry; then treat them with the other cure until they are healed."
"But if a fracture of bone occurs in the ulcer, and it is small, then extract it with a gesture [i.e., manual manipulation]."
"And I say the cure for fractures of the head in what follows."
"On ulcers of the neck."
"But if an ulcer occurs on the neck, then there is no difference in treatment between that and a simple ulcer of the head."
"But if the nerve of the neck is incised or an artery, then if it is a nerve, there is nothing to do but avoid treatment with anything that causes violent contraction, such as vinegar and vitriol and similar things because they numb the nerves and contract them." "And do not apply to the ulcer any hot thing, because the substance of nerves is hot and their continuity is with the brain, which is the chief part of all members."
"And if it is treated with light medicines, such as a clean wound washed with sweet water, sometimes infused in rose oil or not, and turpentine when washed with sweet water, and other minor stones according to this manner, and moist ointments."
"But if the ulcer is large, then apply stitches or draw together the lips of it and properly treat it with powder until it is healed."
"But if there is depth in the ulcer or some purulent matter has accumulated at its bottom, then pierce it with something pointed and depressed."
"But if an artery is incised in the ulcer and bleeding occurs, then separate it or dry it up, tie it off, or cauterize it if necessity requires it."
"But if the vertebrae of the nape or all are injured and the veins of the neck are saved, then adjust the lips of the wound over the spine of the nape with stitches and do not touch it. Then equalize it and reduce it to its natural figure, properly bind it with suitable strapping, leave it for a few days; then treat it with ointments that have astringent and stimulating properties until it is healed."
"But if the ulcer has already reached the bone, then percuss it. And if there are fragments of bone in it, remove all that you can; and what cannot be removed, leave it until the ulcer purges itself, separates and its inflammation is alleviated."
"And if the ulcer is fresh, then powder it with that powder which I have recommended to you." "But if there is a fistula in the ulcer, then treat it with that of which the memory will come properly in its chapter."
"If it is a wound from a lance or scalpel and its depth is uncertain, then consider whether there is any wind in the ulcer when the patient breathes out. Then know that the ulcer is fatal."
"And if there is no depth and the ulcer is fresh with its own blood, then pause upon it with the first hour powder, and do not bind it tightly so that blood does not remain in its depth and return to the heart and worsen the illness. Rather, pause upon it with attractive ointment and wash it twice a day."
"And if the ointment is not at hand, then pause upon the ulcer with cotton wool so that it may suck up the humidity. And make the patient sleep on the ulcer so that what has been applied to it may run."
"But if three days or more have passed since the ulcer and there has been no spasm or cardiac tremor nor rigidity of breath in the patient, and other conditions are good, then know that the ulcer is safe; then treat it with linseed and other remedies until it heals."
"But if the healing of it is delayed and all time has passed, then know that a fistula has already formed. Therefore treat it according to its chapter."
"And if the ulcer is from an incision with a razor or scalpel and is simple on the surface of the foot or hand, then treat it with what is mentioned for suturing if it is large, or with powder if it is small."
If there has already been injury to the bone, and it is already incised, then twist the tweezers and gently attempt to extract those pieces if they are separated. But if they are not separated, then leave them where they can be easily reached, as their extraction is facilitated that way.
And for other accidental wounds in the other members, the treatment in cure is what we have said
Of the wounds of the belly
The condition that occurs in the belly is either great, small, or moderate.
And a small disruption is done differently, and indeed it is often difficult to reduce them, unless you quickly intervene with their return to their proper place, because they open up and become swollen so that entry becomes difficult.
And therefore the best disruption is moderate, because in that one it is not difficult to reduce the bowel, just as it is difficult in both species mentioned above.
And know that suture of the belly is done in four ways, but two sutures are common and convenient for sutures of other wounds of the body.
And two other sutures are proper to other wounds, but they are more proper in the suture of the belly.
And I recount each one with its exposition and explanation.
Therefore I say that when the wound is small and some cause of the bowel comes out through it, and it is difficult to reduce it, then this is in one of two ways: either because of the smallness of the disruption, as we have said, or when inflammation has come to the bowel due to the coldness of the air.
And therefore when this is the case, then you should scrape it so that you immerse a sponge or moist cloth in hot water alone, or take some water with squinant, and cypripedium, and spikenard, and myrrh with which to moisten the bowel inside until the inflammation is resolved.
And sometimes it is good to use wine in which there is astringency, and it is better than water alone in the resolution of inflammation.
And therefore when you see that the inflammation is resolved, then you moisten the bowel with water in which there is a lot of salt and mallows, because by this means entry becomes easier with little cause.
But if the entry of it is not facilitated by this cure, then cut open the disruption a little with an instrument with which you have made incisions.
And the first cure is better, when it is appropriate that it is not sectioning, and there is no need to cut open unless it is necessary and urgent.
Of the instrument, see its shape:
Sharpen one part of the knife acutely, and make the other part blunt; let its extremity be subtle but not too sharp-pointed.
And therefore when the hernia has been pushed out and comes forth through the anus, it is necessary to reduce it again according to its natural form in its proper place if this can be done.
But when the rupture is wide and situated in the lower part of the belly, then you should make the patient lie on their back and elevate their hips as much as their head; but if it is in the upper part of the belly, then you should elevate the head and feet as high as possible.
Similarly if the rupture is in one of the two sides of the belly, then always have your intention and attention directed towards that side where the hernia is located so that it may be elevated higher than the other.
And this should also be done in both large and moderate hernias.
"In small plagues, pause what is prepared for you, and immediately make your servant be between your hands with his entire disruption held in one hand and adjust the lips of it."
"This is the common method of suturing: take the needle, or count needles according to the amount of the plague's width. Then leave from the extremity of the disruption of the said finger, and thread the needle once, but do not insert it in both extremities of the skin with two knots that remain outside the skin on both sides of the parted flesh, until you have made it penetrate one side; then you have joined both extremities of the skin and both extremities of the ciphac, and now make four stitches."
"And again thread with double thread around the needle, and sometimes take both parts together, and then join both lips and the plague decently. Then leave the thickness of the finger and put another needle in it, and then tie it with thread like you did with the first needle; do not stop doing this if necessary until all the contraction of the plague is complete."
"And the needles should be between thickness and subtlety, for subtle needles hardly cut into the flesh, while thick ones are difficult to insert in the skin. Therefore they should be of medium thickness."
"And it is not necessary that the needle should be inserted in both extremities of the skin too much, lest the flesh be lightly cut and the wound opens before its consolidation."
"And do not make the stitches too long so that the wound is not prevented from healing."
"And then cut the extremities of the needles so that they do not leave the wound open during sleep. Then press over the stitches powdered with a little of each herb, which will hold the extremities of the needles in place; and leave them until you know that the wound has healed."
"And these kinds of stitches with needles are more suitable for small wounds, where one needle or two may be enough to close them."
"But the second stitch is common: it consists of bringing together with a single stitch all four extremities of the skin and both extremities of the cicatrix, using a needle with thread wound around it in subtlety and thickness."
"And when the needle has passed through these four extremities, you will reduce it from that same side where you started, so that the thread draws back and brings the edges of the wound together in order to make a stitch like sewing with cloth, with which the edges are strengthened."
"And pause between each stitch the thickness of the little finger."
"And this stitch is known by men."
"And with this stitch I closed a wound that had been caused by a knife to a man in his belly, which was about two palms long and wide. The disruption of the wound was more than one palm, and it had exited from the bowels around two palms near the middle of the member. And then I reduced that part where the bowel had been torn for 24 hours."
"And the wound was healed in the space of about fifteen days or so, and the man was cured. And he lived many years after that, and is still alive today."
"And other doctors had judged that he would not be healed; and by miracle he was healed, for I did not cure him with any ointment, because it was a place where no ointment nor any medicine could be applied. But I put cotton soaked in honey on the wound twice a day and washed it assiduously with water of honey until it was healed."
"But I will tell you two kinds of stitches proper according to the narrative of the Word."
"And it is that you begin with a stitch on the skin edge, and insert the needle from without inwards."
"And then, as the needle penetrates into the skin and through the length of the wound, leave the extremity of the cicatrix in which you inserted the needle; and make the needle pass through the other extremity from within outwards in the other extremity of the member."
"And then, when you have made it penetrate once more, now make it penetrate again in the same extremity of the member from without inwards; and leave the extremity of the cicatrix that is on this side, and make the needle pass through the other extremity from within outwards."
"And then, with that same penetration with which you made it penetrate into the cicatrix in the extremity of the member which is on the inside so that all may penetrate."
"And then begin from this same side and pass it with the extremity that is of the cicatrix on the other side, and withdraw the needle from the skin which has penetrated, and reduce it in that skin and attach the extremity of the cicatrix which is on the other side with this extremity of the member; and withdraw it from the skin which is on its part."
"And do this once or twice, and do it once again just as often until you close all that is without according to that example."
"And the sum of this manner of stitching is that you close with a skin stitch by which you close the skins, so that you close the cicatrix once from the side of the skin edge, and leave it open again until it has completed."
"But the second kind of proper stitching which you have especially taught by Galen, and this is of speech concerning it and recommended by him, is that you close it in such a way that certain physicians close it, namely, bringing together all parts to their corresponding parts by nature; and they apply the extremity of one part to the extremity of another part, and this is done according to what I have told you."
"That you leave the needle in the extremity of the member near the skin edge, and make it penetrate from without into the depth of that same place, leaving two extremities of the cicatrix; and then turn the needle round and make it penetrate again from without to within through both extremities of the cicatrix."
"And then turn those loops round, and make them penetrate from the depth outwards in two extremities of the member on the opposite side."
"And this manner of stitching is better than common loose stitching, and it is the stitching of which I have previously spoken."
"This is the speech of Galen"

"About the cure of dropsy and fistula."
"Know that every plague and abscess when it opens and becomes old, and is not consolidated and gives off branch at all times and is not cut off, then in whatever member it may be, universally it is called a fistula, and we call it that ulcer."
"And according to truth, a fistula is a hard, thickened, white nodosity without pain and with a cavity like the cavity of the navel, for this reason it is called that ulcer which is wrinkled, that is, a navel."
"And in some men it is humid and gives off branch, and when the humidity is cut off within certain hours." "And sometimes this humidity is much, little, thick, and subtle."
"I have already shown all those causes and their symptoms and the cure of them with medicines in the division."
"And it is proper that I should make you know the cure of fistula which is done by way of operation with a knife."
"And know that fistulas occur in all the members of the body because there are those which pertain to large veins and arteries, or nerves, or the caecum, or the bowel, or the bladder, or to some of the ribs, or to some of the vertebrae of the back, or to certain composite joints by composition like that of the hand and foot; and there are those which give nearness to the principal member and similar ones."
"And therefore those fistulas among these which are in this manner are of difficult healing and do not receive cure, and occupation with them is labor and ignorance."
"And if there is among the fistulas one whose depth does not pertain to those places which have been mentioned and you hope and trust in its cure and want to come to knowledge of it, then take a probe of earth or iron, and if the fistula goes straight, examine it with that."
"But if the fistula is tortuous then investigate it with a probe of subtle lead because lead by its lightness is alleviated and bent according to its tortuousness."
"And if the fistula has many horrific branches, then you cannot examine it with a probe."
"Therefore dilate the horrific part by its horrific parts because the moisture with which you dilate that will go down towards the other horrific parts and their course."
"Then inquire carefully, according to the manner you are able, whether here has been bone or nerve damage. Determine if this is a distant or nearby fistulous cavity, or if it is a fistula with multiple openings and lies beneath all of this with great force, upon which you must examine the patient's complaint with your full strength. With this examination comes the discovery of pain, which you should compress with your hand over the area."
"And when you have examined all those causes with true steadfastness, then proceed to the treatment with confidence: first consider whether it is an apparent fistulous opening near the skin surface or far from joints, nerves, arteries, veins, or any other location you find."
"Then dry the fistula according to what is mentioned earlier about its nose, and remove that which comes from this filtration of rotting flesh, and that which is born in the opening of the flesh or of the skin and similar things. And cure that wound so it may be healed."
"But if the fistula is of great depth and runs perpendicularly, then you must insert your finger into it as far as possible and carefully clean out all rotting flesh. Then apply compresses soaked in acrid medications and place them inside the cavity of the fistula which you have grasped with forceps."
"And do this several times until the acrid medication has eaten away all the rotting matter in the cavity of the fistula."
"Then restore it with dressings that produce healthy flesh, until it is healed."
If it is not healed by this, then cauterize according to what we have recommended.
If the cause of the fistula is bone and it needs to be certified, then you should explore that and dry it according to what we have recommended, provided that you do not harm any vein, nerve or main member as we have said.
If the bone is exposed and there is some corruption and blackness in it, then remove that flesh within which the corruption has spread entirely; afterwards treat it with this medicine until it heals.
And if it does not heal and casts out flesh like before, then know that you have not yet reached the complete eradication of corruption; therefore uncover carefully and examine the cause of it and cleanse it with your last strong remedy, and afterwards restore it.
And if it is healed, then good! But if not, know that the corruption is in the deep and remote parts of the body which you have not reached, and therefore you should leave it alone.
And if the corrupted bone is small or can be extracted, then extract it using fine forceps suitable for that purpose.
And if there are many bones, then carefully extract all of them and do not leave any cause untreated with your strength.
But if there is any difficulty in removing the cause due to humidity or flesh or sinews, and there is no fear of error in its incision, then incise those membranes or flesh if it is nearby, and deliver the bones.
But if you are not able to extract the bones at that time, then close over the wound with a dressing that will keep it moist. Leave that in place until that dressing is absorbed and the wound becomes consolidated and healed around those flesh and sinew-covered bones. Keep the dressing clean so that it does not become contaminated.
When dressing the wound, if you fear infection, cover it with a dressing soaked in olive oil or Egyptian pitch or one of the green ointments, for when those membranes are dried out, they will separate from the bones and their attraction will be alleviated.
But if it is a large bone such as the thighbone or shinbone, or one similar to them, and only the surface of that bone is corrupted, then scrape off that corruption with a curette until all the blackness and corruption have been removed, and then restore the dressing.
But if a large portion of the bone is corrupted and the corruption has already reached the marrow of the bone, then it is necessary to close that wound and incise it at the location where the corruption has penetrated. Then treat it until it becomes consolidated.
And I recommend to you that when operating on the thighbone of a man, you use this model and assistant for your operation.
A young man of about 30 years old presented with pain in his thigh. Upon examination, it was found that the inflammation was on the inside of the thigh and had extended to the bone. There was a large abscess with no external cause.
And so the time was prolonged with that patient due to the error of the physicians, who allowed the abscess to become hard. Much material had accumulated and its treatment was suspended until the thigh became fistulated. Then it was opened in many orifices from which pus and humidity of the body were discharged.
And so he consulted many doctors for about two years, but none of them knew how to treat it except me when I was called.
And I saw the thigh of that man and the material that flowed from those orifices for a great distance. The whole body of that man was already emaciated, and his complexion was yellowish.
And then I inserted a probe into one of those orifices and reached the bone with it.
And then you examined all the orifices carefully and found that they originated from all parts of the thigh.
And then I took courage and incised one of those orifices until I discovered a portion of the bone. I scraped off that corrupted part, which was earthy, corrupt and discolored. And already a portion of the thigh had been corrupted, having penetrated into the muscle
Then I removed what was discovered and prepared from the bone that was uncovered
And you should have seen that it was not in the bone but only that corruption which you could excise and remove, as I had already removed.
Then he paused the dressing with a consolidation medicine for a long time, but it did not consolidate.
And then I reopened and discovered over the bone again according to the first discovery, and scraped off continuous corruption from the bone.
And I removed what appeared to be that corruption, and yet you continued with the treatment and dressing of him, but it was not restored.
And then you discovered it again and did not cease incising the bone little by little particularly and continuing the dressing of him, but it was not restored until you incised about a palm's length, and then you extracted it with the marrow itself, and then you restored it with medicines, and it healed lightly and became consolidated.
It was not necessary to repeat the operation and section of that thigh except for the disposition of his fearlessness and lack of tolerance, and his fear of death. For he was lazy in all hours due to excess evacuation and fainting badly.
Therefore, he healed completely, and there was nothing in place of hard bone and flesh. He had good posture in his body and regained his strength; he changed his ways and did not cause any harm or damage in any way during walking nor by any cause.
But if the bone is prominent in place of the flesh and causes pain, then you should cut it off in this manner: Take a strong thread and tie it at the end of that prominent bone, and command someone to stretch it out. Then take another piece of thick thread and bind it over the first one. Then bind it over the flesh that is next to the bone, stretching the end of it so that you draw the flesh aside from those parts where you want to cut, and then uncover the flesh over the place where you want to saw so that the saw does not touch the flesh.
And place a piece of wood or a board beneath the bone neatly, for when you do this, it prevents the saw from cutting into the corrupt flesh.
The sawing should be done little by little over the corrupt place, because if there is corruption in the concavity of the bone and it does not appear in its appearance to the touch, it may be necessary to saw again later.
But if the bone is corrupted but not prominent, then it means that one part is continuous with another, and the corruption is in the middle or in a part of it. In this case, you should uncover all the flesh from every side; place wood beneath it, and saw off the first part where the corruption begins so that the sawing continues to the other part.
The sawing should be done according to the location of the corruption, as we have said.
And if the corruption is at the joint, incise that corrupted joint and saw off the bone where it continues with the other part.
But if the corruption is in the continuity of two joints, then it is not in reason but by chance.
And if the corruption is in the palm of the hand or foot, it is very difficult. But you should investigate how the corruption appears to you and abrade it accordingly, cleaning it according to each position that is possible for you, so that with each operation, you rectify what you can without harming any vein or nerve.
