"This is how the chapter of the third part begins: On restoration." "This chapter is often necessary in the art of medicine, and concerns the restoration of fractures and accidents of dislocation in bones."
"Oh, son, know that those who boast of this chapter are foolish doctors and the common people, and those who have never regarded the books of the Ancients nor read a single letter from them."
"And because of this reason it happens that this method is private among the learned in our land; and I have not forgotten this during all my life; nor have I added what follows except for the length of reading the books of the Ancients and study over their understanding, so as to extract science from them."
"And therefore I direct this to experiment and study throughout my life's duration."
"And I have already described in this chapter all that my science contains and about which my experiment has passed; since here I have briefly for you others and relieved myself of the occupation of lengthening it; and abbreviated it to the point of abbreviation; and shown it to the point of declaration; and formed many forms of instruments administered therein, as is done by addition of declaration, as I have done in two preceding chapters."
"Chapter One."
"Summaries and collections of the essence of bone fractures, from which a preamble is necessary before we begin to remember the members that are broken and diverse if each one." "It is therefore necessary that I say in the prologue of this chapter the summaries of the discourse, and the chapters under whose understanding the artisans are forced to stay at rest over their truth, so that you and those who are eager may know or learn this noble art besides you."
"And therefore I say that when some fracture or dislocation or torsion or concussion happens, it is necessary at the beginning to bleed that one with flebotomy or to alleviate him, or whatever ensemble you may prohibit any cause, as debility of virtue, or if he is a child or old in decrepitude, or if it is time of violent heat or cold."
"And therefore it is necessary to content him with his food of fresh herbs and meat of birds and crayfish; and prohibit gross meats and full viandas and all vianda that fills the veins with blood, so that you may be sure of avoiding an abscess, and if you are not afraid of effusion of matter in the member."
"And therefore it is necessary to return the patient to his first regimen with which he was accustomed to live."
"And therefore when the bone begins to be restored, it is necessary that the patient be nourished with nutrient foods rich and heavy in which there is viscosity, such as foods made of wheat cooked with water, rice, chicken, eggs, beef, butter, fresh fish, thick wine, and similar ones; and with this regimen coagulation of fracture is performed more easily and better."
"And know that the bones of those who are in their first vigor and those who are old do not have it possible for them to be joined and consolidated according to their nature due to the dryness of their members."
"But nonetheless nature adjusts and adapts itself to the bone broken in any part whatsoever, as glue that adheres and strengthens it insofar as it is conglutinated and united together, until the end of fortitude and firmness comes, as fire at the beginning, insofar as no cause prohibits it from its works."
"And for this reason it is necessary that you suspend the patient's foods, foods in which there is gravity and viscosity and thickness, as we have said."
"And know that the species of fractures are diverse according to the diversity of members, for a fracture of the bone of the leg is different from the fracture of the bone of the head; and the fracture of the bone of the foot is different from the fracture of the bone of the hand; and similarly all other members are diverse among themselves."
"And I come with a reminder of every species of fracture, explained in its own chapter distinguished from the others."
"Great diversity of fracture within itself." "And the species of fractures of the bone are diversified within themselves; for an arched fracture occurs in some other way than stellar ones do; and sometimes it is made according to the length of the bone: these are the fractures of the satagia and stars and additional ones that occur and do not occur, as happens in the pen when it is prepared to write, and a fracture with plugging and disruption in the head, and a small fissure fracture."
"And to each species there is a proper pain or torment in its restoration, according to what will come later the reminder of it distinguished in its places."
"Great sign of fracture of the bone." "And for this reason a fracture of the bone is known: its tortuosity and prominence and depression at touch, and it also happens when you press it with your hand."
"And therefore when there is no apparent tortuosity nor does it happen that any pain is made upon touching the bone with agitation and the patient does not find great pain, then there is no fracture here."
"It is possible that there may be tortuosity or a small fissure, therefore you should not move it with your hand and compress everything; rather pause upon it regarding the medicines of which reminder will be made at that moment which is convenient for the place; and especially let there be straightening through subtle restriction."
"And know that the place where it is arched and not split into two or other than stellar ones do, since all its parts are removed, then you should begin at your hour to straighten it insofar as an abscess may happen."
"And if an abscess occurs there, then leave it for some days until the abscess stops; and then splint it according to each manner that you can of elevation and binding."
"And know that restoration of it and binding of it is easier than the bone in which stellar ones occur."
"And then strengthen it according to what will come the reminder of it."
"But if there are stars in the bone, then it is necessary to extend this broken member in two parts, if it is the hand or foot; or with your hand if the member is small; or with two strings or with one string and your hand."
"The position you take to pause the affected limb should be according to its natural shape, while the parts are extended. Then you should endeavor to return those additions with ingenuity and according to each manner that you can in their places."
"And be careful, according to your knowledge, not to cause harm to the patient through your operation of their limbs or injury. And endeavor, according to your ability, to apply one bone with another in its best forms."
"And it is necessary that at that opportune time you touch him with your hand firmly, and if any different cause arises, correct him and equalize him according to the quantity of your strength."
"And avoid violent extension and compression, as many foolish doctors do who often cause an abscess to cauterize by their operation or cause contraction in the limb, as we have seen happen too often."
"And the quality of compression for a limb in repose is that which I recommend, because you should know that broken limbs are diverse in their smallness and greatness, and in their shape."
"And for those that are small, like the arm, hand, and foot, and similar ones, it is necessary that the wrapping be of very fine cloth."
"But for those that are large, like the thigh or calf, it is necessary to have a wrapping that is thick and strong, because tight wrapping connects the limb well and compresses it equally on all sides, so that nothing enters inside."
"And at the beginning, begin your compression of the dressing, pause appropriately according to what is necessary, over the location of that fracture with three wrappings or four, depending on how much the limb deserves it. And tighten your hand a little with the bandage."
"And when you have wrapped up to that point beyond the highest part of the fracture location, then tighten more so that your compression is not lessened by what you have used to compress the fracture site." "And then extend yourself with wrapping from the fracture site a little way, and gradually loosen the compression until you reach about half the length of the limb."
"And then take another bandage and wrap it again over the broken site with wrapping, and extend yourself with wrappings beyond the fracture location. And your operation should be in tightening the wrapping and loosening it gradually according to what was said earlier about the first wrapping."
"And then pause between the wrappings with a layer of wool or cloth with which to equalize any twisting of the fracture, if there is any twisting in it. And otherwise, do not pause for any reason."
"And then pause over the other bandage, and then press down upon those wrappings carefully made at that time, if there is no inflammation or cauterized abscess in that location. And otherwise, pause over what covers the abscess or inflammation; and leave it like that for a few days. And then tighten over those wrappings."
"And the splints should be made of thin, fine reeds, well-shaped; or they can be made of wood from a fig tree, or branches of palm. And each splint should be about this width and shape:"
"However, the splint placed over that fracture should be thicker and wider than the other splints, and its length should be proportional to the size of the limb. And then tighten over those splints with another bandage according to your first compression; and then lay above it with felt carefully, according to what was said about compression, so that your compression is stronger at the fracture site, and as it goes further from the fracture, the compression should be lessened."
"And the threads should be medium in thickness and fineness, and made of wool. And if the threads are thick, like those used by some foolish people who use cords of twisted flax for their operations, it is a great error when compression is uneven."
"And very fine threads are not suitable, because you will not achieve compression with them as you wish. And there should be less than one finger's breadth between splint and splint."
"And if the pain is felt at the ends of the splints near the compression in those places, then pause with wool or carded lamb's wool between them until it stops hurting."
"Sometimes a fracture occurs with plague, and disruption of the knee joint happens. I have spoken about this in Chapter 18 on singularities."
"And know that not all broken limbs are bound with splints on the first day; because when a limb is large, you should wait for about 5 or 7 days, or more, according to your certainty of the appearance of a cauterized abscess before binding it with splints."
"And this is the method used by plasterers to restore fractures, dislocations, and distortions."
"The method of plastering commonly used for the restoration of fractures is convenient for many complications, especially for women and children because it does not bring heat or friction. And you should take powder from a millstone; that is, the bran left over after the movement of the millstone against the walls of the mill."
"And it is ground like that, but sifted with chalk and water, and used thickly and coarsely."
"Another method of plastering for the restoration of fractures, dislocations, and distortions: take assafetida, ladanum, acacia, enula, and miguel, which is the resin of wild mastic, all of them ground and sifted and mixed with aloe or chalk water if the condition is scaly. And use this plaster because it has a good composition and restores lightly broken bones, suitable for many people."
"Another method of plastering that suits fractures and distortions: Take miguel resin and white wax, each one dragma x; myrrh and aloe each dragma v; acacia dragma vi; and Armenian bole dragma xx."
"All of these are ground together in a mortar with great care, sifted, mixed with water or chalk water, and applied."
"Method of plastering for joints and separated bones in their locations, and also useful for pain accidentally occurring in those areas, as well as for cracks and fractures: Take clean wool, immerse it in vinegar and cooked oil, and apply it to the location."
"But this plaster does not have the virtue of restoration nor does it prevent or cure cauterized abscesses and does not properly expel pains."
"Another method of plastering that restores broken bones: Take leaves from wild fig trees and poppy pods, mix them together and make a plaster out of them."
Mix in equal parts of ale root, chamomile flowers, and violet flowers. Add ground iris flour until thickened. If the limb is not scratched, boil with wine and apply as a plaster for bone fracture restoration or to resolve remaining abscesses.
If it is scratched, then soak it in coriander water made wet with water and apply the plaster.
Another method of plaster that is even stronger in resolving than the first one when an abscess occurs during bone restoration: Take equal parts of ale root and seed, balm, melilot, marjoram, violet flowers, chamomile, and ground iris. All are crushed and cooked with willow water or sweet water or wine, according to the quality of the limb and the rest of its color.
However, Hippocrates does not specify in his book that it should be plastered on the broken limb during its restoration, but rather recommends a cerote made of wax and oil, and nothing else. He advises that it should be medium-soft between thickness and subtlety.
However, it is seen that in Galen's book, plasters are applied on the broken limb during its restoration and the causes of drying with slight heat are such as myrrh, frankincense, and similar ones for those same causes that we have recommended.
Method of plaster that relieves injury and pain: Take equal parts of niter, cypress, human hair cuttings or bird feathers, and ale root. All are ground together and sifted, then cooked to make the plaster.
However, if the amount according to how much remains of the plaster and it is appropriate to be loosened, it should be considered that if there is no pain or swelling nor has the bone broken, then it should not be removed too soon. And if there is severe pain or swelling or inflammation at the location, then you should remove it immediately in that hour and take off the plaster from it.
And then take some linen and a wet sea sponge, immerse it in hot water, and with that wash the area inside until the swelling stops and the pain is relieved. Then let the limb rest for an hour. Then plaster it again with wool submerged in sour wine and oil or rose oil, laying it on top of it for one night to ensure the formation of a dry abscess and that the inflammation of the limb has subsided and its pain has disappeared.
And then repeat that gentle strapping with a small plaster again, not tightening too much the first time, and gently until it is healed.
And if at some point the abscess, redness, pain, inflammation, and all other accidents have disappeared completely, then you should again plaster and strap it as you did at the beginning.
And if no cause of those that we have mentioned appears on the limb, then you should not remove it before three days or four or five or seven. And sometimes all this is left for twenty days according to what you see through the loosening of the limb, as we have said, until the fracture adheres and joins together with the coagulation of the flesh above it.
And then adjust the strapping more tightly than the first time, according to how much the swelling of the limb has decreased by remembering it.
But if you find the location of the fracture already dry and shrunken more than it should be, then know that nutrition is prohibited from reaching it. Therefore, immerse it every day that you remove the plaster with hot water until all is done in three days. Then loosen the strapping a little, and through this operation the limb receives nourishment and heals easily.
But what foolish restorers of the fracture of the limb do again when it was not restored at first, according to how it should have been restored, and is restored crookedly, that is an error according to their operation, and great fear. And if they were right, the Ancients would have enlarged upon this in their books and practiced it.
And I have not seen any of them do that, which is why it is more reasonable that they should not do it.
Chapter Two: On fracture when it occurs in the head.
Fracture types are many, and their appearances are diverse, and their causes are distinct. For some fractures are caused by blows that break all the bone within the cavity, leaving the bone like a log, thus this type is called compression; or the blow cuts some part of the bone without cutting deep into the flesh; and this type of fracture is called avulsion.
The plague of these two fractures is either great or small, and it occurs in compression or tension fractures. The cause of this is a blow accompanied by force or impact on bone, and they appear similar to each other.
This fracture is either penetrating, nearly reaching the surrounding soft tissue around the bone, or it occurs on the surface of the bone. The severity of these two fractures can be either great or small.
And there is a fracture that occurs within the bone itself, in its smallest detail resembling a hair's breadth, and thus this type of fracture is called capillary fracture.
There is also a fracture that occurs from impact or blow by a hard object which causes some of the bone layers to enter into the inner parts, and this happens at the concave location like what happens to eggshells when they are struck and their inner parts break open.
This usually occurs in the bodies of human bones, especially the heads of long bones such as femurs or tibias.
And therefore all these species of fractures appear like stars separated by lines. And I have brought all this to your attention in its proper place.
Know all these types of fracture by their detection on the surface and by inquiring about them through touch and examination of corrupted flesh around them.
Capillary fractures are recognized by the fact that they appear as small dark lines on the surface of the bone, which can be wiped clean and felt with the fingertips. These lines appear black when examined closely.
"In treating fractures, consider initially the patient's symptoms. If you find them significant due to manifest fear, such as vomiting from cholera, diarrhea, loss of consciousness, fainting spells, high fever, discharge from the eyes and ears, and similar symptoms in severe conditions, then healing is doubtful."
"If you find non-life-threatening symptoms, then proceed with treatment."
"If the patient comes to you already injured, especially if it happened during winter days around December 14th, then you should examine the bone before treating according to proper procedure."
"If it happens during summer days, then you should examine the bone on the 7th day, ensuring that the surrounding flesh is not corrupted and that symptoms as recommended have occurred."
"If the fracture has already reached the muscular tissue covering the brain and is in pieces with attrition, then you should insert the comminuted and attritional fragments as I will instruct you."
"And after shaving the head of the patient, uncover the bone according to your custom and ability following the shape of the injury, making it easy for the patient."
"If you detect the bone through skin wounds or pus accumulation, then pause your procedure according to what is necessary. If you see fluid oozing from the wound, place cloths soaked in wine and rose oil on it until the inflammation subsides and blood flow ceases. Then begin with the circumcision of the bone and its removal."
"And this is according to one of two operative methods."
"And one method is that you insert the bone with a narrow-bladed incision knife, and this is its shape: Then administer another incision knife, slightly wider than the first one, and this is its shape:"
"And then use an even wider incision knife."
"And you should have different types of incision knives available in various sizes, some wider than others and some shorter than others with sharp tips made of iron or steel."
"Use them skillfully to strike the incision without hitting the head or hurting the patient."
"If the bone is hard and tough, then you should first drill around it before administering the incision knives with the handles, which are called non-penetrating handles."
"However, these are not called non-penetrating handles but rather those which do not penetrate the end of the bone at that point where it is about to be cut. For the handle has a round tip or edge on that side which is left behind with an acute head similar to a small margin or circle, which prevents being submerged and passing through the thickness of the bone."
"And it is convenient for you to take many of these handles, each suitable for the thickness of each bone. So that you may have them ready during all surgeries on the brain, with tips that are sharp and their length and width according to the thickness of the bone."
"And this is the shape of three types of handles:
E approprincipitally cut with scissors what is between each trace of the bone, and collect that subtle pus which you can gather at the end of the incision, as we have said, before you remove the bone, either with your hand or with some other cause through certain instruments which you shall have prepared for this purpose, such as tweezers and forceps.
E que tu gardas que l'inscissori non tocave null'am causa del ciphac.
E donc cum auras circuyt lo os perforat, e sera separat del ciphac si es continuat amb ell, e auras aquell extrayt, covent et engualhar aquel en la asperitat de lu de aquo que es en lo os amb altra esturment semblante a inscissori, però covent que sia pus teon i pus subtil que las altres incisors.
E si roman alguna partida dels ossos petitos i satajas, llavors pren aquella amb facilitatz amb aqüell que és deves tu dels esturments; e aprop aquò cures la plaça amb liniments i engueix els quals havem dit.
E l'altra manera d'operació és més lleugera i llunyana del pau, la qual diu Galien i l'elogia per gran elogista; i aquest és el seu discurs: és necessari que al principi comencis a discoverir la partida de l'os del lloc on hi ha la fractura més visible i evident, entre que quan haves descobert aquella partida i haves posat de banda l'extremitat d'aquest incisi lent i alegre, el qual aquesta és la seva forma:
E és que la partida lenticular és llarga i no incideix cap causa, i la partida aguda és de l'altura de les dues cares davants en longitud, així com es veuen; que la partida lenticular sia ajustada al ciphac amb la cara de l'incisió aguda a l'os.
E després, percuteu sobre l'incisi d'una partida amb un petit martell fins a tallar tot l'os amb facilitat com si girés, i tu estàs segur que el panicul no es podrà produir en aquest accident que temis de cap manera; i si el metge era el més bo de tots els homes i el més ignorant d'ells; certament, i si fos dormit.
Ara, si hi ha cap causa annexada a l'os del panicul en alguns llocs de l'os, talla aquella amb l'extremitat d'aquest incisi lenticular i allibera-la d'aquell lloc amb facilitat, perquè és alliberada d'aquell lloc sense nociu ni por.
I si la fractura de l'os no penetra dins el panicul i allò que es abscis o es ostenta de l'os és la seva cara i roman amb aspereza i petites arrugues, llavors cal que arrasin aquesta aspereza i erradiquin aquestes arrugues amb rasuradors subtils dels quals tu has preparat diversos nombres de diferents quantitats perquè sigui possible aplicar-ne els més convenients en tot lloc segons allò on haures d'actuar aquest os trencat i la seva figura.
I cal que administres en la teva raó quan rasures l'os al principi els més amples dels rasuradors i després els més subtils, i no deixis de fer-ho fins a arribar a aplicar els més aguts i subtils de tots.
però en cadascuna d'aquelles altres petites fractures capil·lars i fractures subtils, cal que apliques cura segons com tu has trobat per rectificar-ho.
I és que no s'ameguda sobre tots aquells que han estudiat en aquesta art. I això és el que hem demostrat en fractures grosses.
I si el panicul es reté quan tu erradiquis l'os, llavors cal que agafis drap segons la quantitat de la ferida, i sumergis aquell en oli rosat, i posis-lo sobre la ferida; després pren altre drap doble o triple, sumerge'l en vi i oli rosat i posar-ho sobre un drap prim, i fa-ho tan lleugerament com puguis sobre aquest, sense gravar el ciphac; i finalment aplica-hi un liant gruixut, però no estrenyis aquest només per la quantitat que tinga la ferida.
I deixeu-lo amb aquesta cura durant un dia o dos fins que esteu segur de l'apostema caut; després, retireu-ho i aplica-hi medicaments per evitar la siccitat excessiva, com ara raig d'oli de llinos i farina d'erba, pols d'henna i aristologia, i altres semblants.
I també pots fer d'aquestes medines pols que s'hagin aplicat a la ferida de manera seca; i, generalment, aplica-hi tots els medicaments que tinguin la propietat d'aseure i no de morder.
I mantingues la netedat de la ferida durant el teu tractament i no deixis que s'omple de suc o d'altres coses; i no deixis que es faci virulent a causa del teu tractament, perquè quan s'aplica sobre el panicul del cervell, es corromp i pot produir malaltia greu.
I també pot ocórrer al ciphac del cap quan l'os és mondificat i més encara quan se'n fa el tractament; i si això passa, apareixerà una negror a la superfície d'aquest. En aquest cas, si s'han produït les accidents que hem mencionat abans, sabràs que ha estat un cas fatal.
I si no s'hi ha produït negror, sinó només a causa de la medicina aplicada sobre ell, és per la virtut d'aquesta medicina que s'ha produït aquesta negror. En aquest cas, cal prendre una part de mel i tres parts d'oli rosat i barrejar-los amb un drap; després posar-lo sobre el ciphac i aplicar-hi especis de curació fins que s'hagi guarir.
Capítol III
Quant al capítol III, parla de la restauració del nas quan es trenca. Es diu que si el nas es trenca per una caiguda o un cop, cal aplicar-li una cura immediata perquè no es produïsca una ferida profunda. Si això passa, s'ha de netejar la ferida amb aigua bullent i després es pot aplicar un liant fet amb mel i vi. També es recomana l'ús d'unes pinces per estirar els extrems trencats del nas fins que s'ajunten perfectament; després cal aplicar-hi una bèndiga feta de llana impregnada en oli d'oliva, i fixar-la amb un cotó. Finalment, es diu que cal esperar tres dies abans de retirar la bèndiga.
Has de saber que el nas no es trenca en dues parts alhora i més amunt, ni en una d'aquelles ocasions que es veuen, perquè té dos ossos. La partida esquerra és cartilaginosa i no es trenca; només hi ha atricció, tortuositat i sinuositad.
però si es trenca una de les dues parts del nas, llavors cal introduir el dit petit dins del seu nas i empènyer l'os trencat amb el dit índex i el dit polze de fora, fins que el nas torni a tenir la seva forma natural. I cal fer això amb cura i evitant causar dolor al pacient.
I si la trencada és a l'os més alt del nas i el dit petit no arriba fins allí, cal usar l'extremitat d'un ganxo de mida petita.
I si la trencada afecta les dues parts del nas, llavors hauràs de fer el mateix que s'ha descrit anteriorment.
Cal actuar amb rapidesa per a restaurar el nas en el primer dia després de la trencada, si és possible. Si no ho és, cal esperar fins que s'hagi resolt l'hematoma i aplicar un cataplasma càustic.
"And then insert into the tip of the nose a cloth plug, if the fracture is in one part, or two cotton balls, if it is in two parts, and ensure that the cloth or cotton is thick enough to fill the passage of the nose."
"And some of the ancient restorers say that cotton balls should be wrapped in linen and changed throughout the day, but this is not visible to me; however it is necessary that the plug should be inserted into a piece of alum and powdered myrrh."
"And sometimes it is placed in the nose instead of cotton, a piece of linen wrapped around it; even if there is strong fracture and inflammation preventing inspiration."
"If this method is not necessary for you, then do as you wish, or use cotton balls instead."
"And if there occurs another time an abscess, plaster the nose with wax or fat melted in vinegar and rose oil, or with diacylon."
"But if there is no abscess formed, then you should plaster it from the outside with sesame flour and powdered myrrh mixed with alum; and also apply a piece of linen soaked in warm water over it, without ever washing the nose for any reason."
"But if the bones of the nose are broken with a small fracture or crushed, then it is necessary to split them and extract those that are convenient using appropriate instruments; afterwards stitch up the wound and treat it with something that incises and consolidates, along with appropriate plasters."
"And if there is inflammation inside the nose, then it should be treated with cotton balls and lead cannulae administered until it is completely healed."
"Chapter Four."
To restore the jaw when it is clenched.
If the jaw is broken but the fracture does not extend across the entire length and is not displaced or fragmented inward, then its reduction will be easy.
Therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, insert your left index finger into the patient's mouth and do likewise for the left side with your right index finger. Push the bone fragment gently inward with your finger until it aligns properly with the outer edge of the bone using your other hand.
And if the jaw fracture is already separated into two parts, then you must apply traction to both segments in a straight line until it is possible to achieve reduction.
And if teeth are loosened or displaced due to the fracture, then strengthen those that you expect will break with a gold, silver, or copper wire. Then apply pressure on top of the broken jaw using cotton wool, folded linen, and a large sterilized gauze pad or a piece of wood cut to the length of the jaw. Then tie it securely in place according to your preference and the situation at hand, ensuring that it is not too tight as to cause further damage.
Tell the patient to rest quietly, avoiding any movement of the jaw for about 10 days and to eat soft foods that do not require chewing.
If you notice that the jaw's position has changed in any way after the reduction, then on the third day, carefully remove the splint and check if there is any alteration in the fracture site. Then pack it with iodoform gauze or plaster of Paris powder mixed with egg albumen or flour paste, ensuring to remove the cotton wool first. Finally, apply a bandage made of linen around the jaw.
And when the splint is securely attached and the position of the bone has not changed, leave it in place until the fracture has healed and strengthened. Often, this type of fracture takes about three weeks to heal completely.
And if an abscess forms later on, then apply warm compresses frequently to the affected area until the abscess is drained and healed. You may also use hot saline rinses to help relieve pain and promote healing.
And if the fracture is comminuted (i.e., broken into multiple fragments), then determine whether there are any loose bone fragments or bone spicules present. If so, carefully remove them using appropriate instruments designed for this purpose.
If the wound from the fracture is large, then you should widen it further with a curette or rongeur according to your needs. This will allow for better visualization of the fracture site and facilitate reduction.
And once you have removed any loose bone fragments, if there is still some debris present in the wound, then carefully clean it out. If the wound is large, pack it with gauze soaked in an antiseptic solution or a suitable dressing and apply a bandage to keep it in place until healed.
Chapter Five
On the restoration of the clavicle when it is broken.
The clavicle (collarbone) is often fractured near its midshaft and this fracture typically occurs in one of three patterns:
Broken or separated, or in two parts without accidents; it is easy to restore. Or thus: it occurs with accidents and is difficult to restore.
In that case, when the fracture is without plague, you should have two ministers present: one to hold the assistant and follow the broken clavicle, and the other to extend the neck towards that part.
And appropriately align the fracture with these said tongs so that it may be made according to its proper shape, and without prominence or concavity.
And if you need more extension, then it is necessary that you place on the skin of the patient's chest a compress made of cloth or wool, and its size should be according to your need; extend and raise the clavicle and compress the compress with your hand while aligning the fracture as needed.
And if you cannot bring the end of the clavicle to the outside edges of the fragments, then it is necessary that the patient be raised up on their back and place a bolster made of cloth or wool under the shoulder muscle.
And the assistant presses on the muscles at the fragments from below until the bone of the clavicle is raised up to its proper position; then you can correct the fracture and align it with the tongs mentioned earlier.
But if you feel resistances from the clavicle and they are painful, then it is necessary that you cut over that area and remove those resistances with ease.
And if it is retained in the bone, then you should perform an incision on it with one of the scalpel blades that you have prepared for this purpose, as you had placed over the clavicle the instrument called "cophac"; and this instrument is made of wood or iron, and its shape is like this:
And it resembles a spoon whose concavity looks like this; and its width should be according to your need, according to the size of the bone or the prominence of it.
But its length should be according to what is possible for you during the operation; that it has two extremities like this, and one extremity is wide and the other is narrow.
And if the disruption of the skin wound that you made when removing the bone resistance is large and you are sure about causing an abscess, then you should approximate the two lips of the wound with suture.
And if the disruption is small or you fear causing an abscess, then you should fill the wound with dressings and powders according to the quantity of the wound opening.
And if an abscess occurs, then you should take a cloth soaked in vinegar oil and rose oil and wine, and place it on that area.
And all of that, make sure the board is not placed on top of the fractured bone; and for this reason it is important that you see the patient every day, so that when you notice that the bandage has softened or observe that the board has already moved out of place, then you should correct that and tighten the binding.
And then place the patient's sleep on the collarbone and place a small pillow below the armpit at night so that the elbow joint is elevated above that side, thereby elevating the clavicle fracture due to the elevation of the muscles, or else the arm should be tied to the neck.
And do not remove the bandage unless you see that it is healed without any wound or abscess, within twelve days.
And then renew the emplaster if you have seen signs of improvement in that area, and adjust it to fit the tightening and restore that and leave it until it has been restored and the clavicle fracture has coagulated.
And it is strengthened and comfortable in about twenty-eight days, although sometimes in some men it may take less time than that.

"The spatula was broken in the right side of his head, but it did not break him, only its edges."

**Chapter VII**
**On the Restoration of Fractures of the Foot.**
"Sometimes the foot is broken in its middle part and then it becomes twisted; but the extremities of it are more often shattered and comminuted."
"And when the middle of the foot is broken, its accidents are that it declines towards the inner parts and there occurs great pain, difficulty in breathing, coughing, fever, and vomiting blood; and it is declared in the cavity of the broken bone and is not concealed from sensation."
"And its restoration is that the wound be elevated on its own back, and a bolster placed between its sole; and properly press the muscles of it, and adjust its sides with the aid of two boards, and perform the operation of it and equalization according to all manner that is possible and convenient for it, until the figure of the bone returns according to what is fitting."
"And then place over it a plaster and gauze, and place over that a thin slice of wood or linen or clay, and wrap that in cloth; and carefully apply that to the broken bone so that it is not moved; and bind it on the back sometimes with a round bandage, and strengthen it with proper constriction; and carefully consider the bandage at all times, and when it is softened, strengthen it."
"And if it is necessary to remove that bandage due to corruption or pressure or pain or abscess that occurs in the place, then carefully cut and loosen that and eradicate the plaster and correct that which has occurred there according to the manner of curing it; and then replace the plaster, sometimes changing its face; and adjust the constriction until it is healed."
**Chapter VIII**
**On the Restoration of Broken Ribs.**
"Know that fracture of the ribs occurs only in thick places on the back, and their extremities at the front part are not subject to such injury except for those that are cartilaginous." "And its recognition is not concealed from sensation by inquiry with them."
"And the reduction should be done thus: align the fracture with these [tools] in the most possible manner, until it is aligned as necessary. Then plaster and strengthen the broken bone with starch if needed."
"If the rib fracture is displaced inward, then it causes severe pain and puncturing, similar to what happens to someone who has been pierced. And because the bone presses on the lung's veil, it makes breathing difficult, coughing, and vomiting of much blood. This condition is difficult to treat."
"And already the Ancients used this for many purposes."
"And they said: whenever foods pause the sick person who generates inflammation and flatulence, so that the abdomen may be inflated and extended, then the fracture will be pushed outward."
"But we would not advise this because it could cause a wound infection if not done carefully. And if an infection has already occurred, then this method will not help."
"And some of them said that it should be paused from above with force and sucked out. And this is like reasoning, but nonetheless it is feared that the suction may draw the superfluities into the lungs due to weakness."
"And some of them said: whenever that place is covered with wool that has already been immersed in hot oil, and powders are paused between the ribs until it is filled up, then let the bandage be tied around as tightly as possible according to its roundness; and let the sick person be treated with food and medicinal cure for pleurisy."
"And if the sick person feels violent constriction that cannot be endured, and the bone pierces the lung's veil with painful punctures, then we fear for the sick person. Therefore, it is necessary to cut over that place, uncover the broken rib, and pause on top of it the instrument that preserves the cyphac, the memory of which has already been given, and gently chisel away the bone and remove it."
"And moreover, if the wound is large, let us join its edges together with stitching, and treat it with plasters until it is healed."
"And if an abscess occurs internally, then quickly and gently pour powders mixed with rose oil onto the place, pause it there, and let the sick person be treated with what is appropriate for internal abscesses."
"And let him lie on his back while sleeping until he is healed."
Chapter ix.
On the restoration of fractures of the spine and neck.
Sometimes fracture occurs in the bones of the neck, and tartar forms on them, similarly to the spine of the back. "And when this happens to someone and you want to know if it will be healed or not, then consider: for if the sinews are already softened, stunned, deadened, and cannot be moved nor extended nor stretched, and when they are constricted with your fingers or pricked with a needle there is no pain felt in them, then you should know that it will not heal, and such conditions are mortal."
"And if they can be moved and feel constriction with your fingers or punctures, then you should know that the nape of the bone is saved and that the illness will improve with treatment."
"And if it looks similar to this in the spine of the neck, and you want to know whether it will be healed or not, then consider its weight."
"Therefore, if you find that the sinews are already softened, it is like what happens in the muscles. And moreover, when he lies on his back, there is difficulty breathing and excretion without his will, and sometimes he lies on his back and wants to urinate but cannot, then you should know that it is mortal, and therefore do not try to treat it."
"But if none of these causes occur, the cause is better and easier to treat."
"And the treatment for this is that you should perforate the seat of the internal abscess that has formed on the broken spine with rose oil alone, or mixed with mulled eggs; pause it there several times a day until the abscess is sedated."
"And then pause one of the comforting and exiting plasters on it, stretch it with linen; and command the sick person to use rest and tranquility and not to sleep on the part where he feels pain until he is healed."
"And if the inflammation of the bone becomes sagging or some cause is already separated from it, then it is necessary to cut over the backbone and remove that bone. And moreover, join the edges of the wound together with sutures if it is large; and moreover treat it with some incensory of herbs until it is healed."
"But if the end of the bone is broken off, and it is the end of the coccyx, then you should insert your left thumb into the anus, and grasp the broken bone with your other hand according to what is possible by equilateral grasping; and moreover pause an emplaster and astel on it, if necessary, and stretch it."
"And if you feel broken sagging bones in it, then cut over that and remove them, and treat the wound with the treatment described earlier until it is healed."
Chapter x.
On the restoration of the ankle joint.
"The bones of the ankles are easily broken, and when they break, their fracture is not other than communion at the ends of them and are inserted in the length and decline towards the inside. And it happens that the sick person feels pain and puncture in that place, and the ankle becomes stunned when there is a fracture."
"And the restoration of this is that you should stretch your hand over it until it covers the fracture which has the figure of it."
"And if the fracture is only at its end, then you should grasp it according to what seems equal to you by grasping until it fits with its natural figure."
"But if the fracture is in the length or has already declined towards the inside, then have the sick person lie on his stomach until you are ready to restore that fracture." "And then when you have grasped it, pause an emplaster on it, and moreover pause a splint of wood or bone on it, and stretch it with great tightness so that there is no fear of puncture from fracture nor removal of the splint."
"And grasp the concavity with whatever fills it until that grasps takes on its proper tightness, and command the sick person to sleep on his back or on his side."
"But if an internal abscess forms, then cease the extension of it and its restoration until the internal abscess is sedated, and pause on it what sedates that according to what has been described earlier. And moreover return to restoring it and stretch it as much as necessary."
"And if the sagging bones or they are comminuted, then remove them."
Chapter xi.
On the restoration of the fracture of the fibula.
"The fibula is that which is between the thighbone and the head of the shoulder."
"And if it is broken, then its restoration is in one of two ways. One way is that you should take a flat piece of wood with medium thickness like this:"
"And let there be tied at both ends two strips of linen, and moreover it should be suspended from the elevated place."
"And have the sick person sit on a chair, and then gently lay his broken arm on the wood so that it reaches its little head in the middle place where the curvature of the wood is attached."
"And then suspend some heavy weight on it, or let the assistant gently extend it towards its outer side. And then the doctor should carefully place the fracture together with his hands until the fracture returns to its proper place."
"The other method is to have the patient lie on their back and suspend the hand of their neck with a bandage. Then command two assistants, one of whom should hold what is above the fracture with their hands, and the other should hold them from below; each extending their part."
"And if you want the extension to be stronger, then stretch over the fracture with a bandage and over that another one. And each of the two assistants should extend their part."
"And if the fracture is close to the extremity of the bone, then half of the bandage should be placed over the joint and the other half below the fracture."
"And likewise if the fracture is close to the elbow, then the bandage should be placed over that spot and over that elbow."
"First of all it should be done with ease and without trouble; and immediately after, if it is tied correctly and applied properly, then stretch that bandage but avoid causing an abscess."
"But if an abscess occurs, then leave the bandage on for about seven days, and apply over it a compress made of thread soaked in vinegar and rose oil until the abscess reappears, and then stretch that bandage."
"And the way to stretch that bandage is as follows: apply a plaster over the fracture, and then apply a wrapping of wet cloth over the plaster; and also adjust the arm over that support, and place their hand wrapped in linen over their muscle."
"And apply the cloth and stretch it over that support for the arm so that the arm is in a still position; unless there is some cause preventing it due to the broken bone."
"And then use splints."
"And then apply over the fracture that splint which should be placed wider and stronger than the others. And place between each splint the width of a finger; and let the length of the splints be according to the fracture, adding three fingers on either side."
"And also stretch over the splints with the bandage which was mentioned at the beginning of this chapter."
"And then your bandage over the location of the fracture should be made tighter." "And as the fracture becomes longer, so the bandage should be looser."
"Therefore, if you arrange the splints and bandage in that way as we have said at the time when you restore the limb to its proper position, then do so."
"And if you fear an abscess occurring, then leave the bandage and splints on for about seven days as we have said."
"And also consider the limb in all cases for three days so that no swelling or inflammation occurs there due to excess bandaging; otherwise, regulate the diet according to what we have said."
"But if you are worried that nothing will happen due to this, then do not remove the bandage but leave it on for at least three days or more."
"And let his sleep be on his back, and let his hands be placed over his stomach."
"And place over the elbow joint a pillow full of wool wrapped in linen. And keep watch at all times during the night or day to ensure that the figure of the broken limb is not displaced nor dissolved, and that the limb remains warm; otherwise, adjust it with your skill."
"And arrange the diet of the patient according to the order which we mentioned earlier. Thus, in the beginning the food should be subtle until it is time to begin stretching [the limb]; and then you must gradually increase the amount of food."
"As for the custom of removing the splint and changing the bandage, this should be done after forty days. And then it is necessary to remove them and administer baths and medications which heal that [injury]."
"If the fracture is already consolidated, then do not remove the bandage from that limb nor the splints for about one day or two months."
"Chapter XII."
"Of the reduction of fractures of the arm."
"The arm is composed of two bones which are called the two elbow bones. Of these, one is small and it lies next to the thumb; the other is large and it lies above the little finger on the outer side."
"And sometimes only the larger elbow bone is fractured, or the smaller one, but often they are both broken together."
"And when the small elbow bone is fractured at its upper end, then its reduction will be easy and its healing will also be quicker."
"But when it [the small elbow bone] is fractured on the outer side, its fracture is severe and its healing difficult."
"And even worse than that is when both bones are broken together."
"Therefore, if the bone that is fractured is the small elbow bone at its upper end, then it is advisable for the physician to perform the reduction with ease and gentleness until that [bone] is completely reduced."
"But if it is the large elbow bone that is broken, then you must perform the reduction with greater force."
"And if both bones are broken together, then you must apply the traction much more forcibly."
"And you must keep the figure of the hand in its proper position during the reduction and traction, extending it upon a couch. And let the thumb be placed above all the other fingers, with the little finger below them, and let the patient lie on a pillow."
"And place a cushion in front of him at his head or feet so that he does not make any effort."
"And then the assistant should extend the arm from the outer side with one hand and hold it there with bandages; another assistant should do likewise above."
"And then the physician should gently reduce the bone until he brings it back to its best position."
"If there is any displacement of the bone in the fracture, then you must carefully reduce all the displaced fragments back into their proper position with your skill."
"If there appears during the reduction any separation of the fragments that pierces the elbow joint and you are not able to reduce it, then proceed as we have said above."
"But if the fracture is accompanied by a wound, I have already spoken about that in another chapter, and therefore I will not discuss its treatment here."
"And if an abscess forms at the beginning of your reduction, then you should take a piece of linen smeared with cerate made from rose oil and white wax, of medium thickness between thickness and thinness. Then stretch this linen over it with gentle pressure until the abscess yields. And then remove the cerate and apply a plaster prepared from powdered myrrh with a little honey; and then apply splints; and let the splint that is placed upon that fracture be wide and strong."
"And know that the number of splints used for the arm is usually greater in the major positions, either when there is a fracture of one elbow bone or of both bones together."
"And then apply the pressure upon the site of the fracture with great force and violence. And if you observe that the traction pulls more on the upper side of the fracture or on the lower side, then apply gentle pressure according to what is written at the beginning of this chapter."
"And let the bandages wrapped around the fracture be soft and moist, not hard or rough. And let the linen with which it is stretched be of medium thickness between thinness and thickness, as we have recommended."
"And observe the limb and the bandage for a few days." "And if any cause arises which requires correction, such as swelling in the limb, then you should immerse the limb in warm water until that swelling ceases."
"Then allow the limb to rest without being too tightly bound so that it may recover; and then apply traction again."
"When the extraction has softened and the bone is dislodged, appearing as such, then correct all this with your power."
"If you find that nutrients are prohibited from reaching the limb due to excess stricture, then soften it a little and allow it to rest for a few days until proper circulation is restored. Then strengthen it."
"However, if the illness has not been caused by any of the conditions mentioned earlier, it should then be released within approximately 20 days or so around that time."
"Then hang the limb of the patient with their own strength and move it agitatedly, and let them sleep on their back."
"Know that this fracture takes 30 or 32 days to heal, but in some cases it heals in 28 days. All of this depends on the complexion and strength of the patient."
"Chapter XIII"
"On the restoration of fractures of the hand and fingers."
"You should know that in the case of a hand pinch or nutcracker injury to the toes, it is not always fracture but often merely contusion."
"And when it occurs with fracture or contusion, you should have the patient lie down and place their hand on that extended [arm] at shoulder height."
"The minister should then extend the broken bones, and the doctor should align them together with good alignment."
"And then you must apply a plaster and cotton, unless there is cauterization to prevent pus. Then place a splint on top according to the size of the location, and wrap it with linen."
"But if the fracture is in the bones within the palm of the hand, you should make a bandage like a splint from cloth and tell the patient to press it with their clenched palm. Then wrap it tightly with cloth."
"And let the splint be made of leather covered in cloth to allow for elevation, so that the leather can adhere closely to the palm. Then bind it as needed."
"But if the fracture is on the outside of the hand, you should place splints above and below the joint of the fingers on the palm to allow the hand to be upright and straight. Then bind it as when wrapping a wrist between the fingers."
"And if there is a fracture in one of the knuckles, you should align it properly and bind it with the palm. And if desired, place a small splint on the outside to elevate the fracture and prevent movement."
"But if the fracture is in another knuckle, such as that of the middle finger or the index finger, ring finger, or little finger, you should align it and bind it with the corresponding finger. Or they can all be bound continuously, which is better. Or place a small splint on top as mentioned for the thumb."
"And consider, in your treatment, the condition of the wound and its opposite side – with your operation – according to what is necessary when such causes arise, as described in this narrative."
"Chapter XIV."
"On the Treatment of a Fracture of the Bone of the Thigh."
"The bone of the thigh is often fractured and it is felt when it becomes displaced at the articulation in front and the pus accumulates above."
"And its treatment is done in such a way that the muscle is bound above the fracture and another bandage is placed below the fracture. And the patient should be made to lie on their stomach and face down."
"And then the minister should extend all the muscles to their insertion when the fracture is in the middle of the bone."
If the thigh is fractured, then bring the ligament close to the bone at the start of the penis, so that the extension goes downwards, and the other ligament leaves the fracture.
Similarly, if the bone is fractured near the knee, then make sure that the ligament is close to the kneecap and the extension goes upwards.
And then the doctor aligns the bone with both his hands so that it reduces naturally and the bone joins together in good union. Afterwards a plaster cast and bandage are needed to prevent swelling of the limb if there is no suppuration (pus formation). But if pus forms then allow it to drain for several days until the inflammation has subsided and then return to your treatment.
The plaster cast should be made in this way: wrap a firm bandage over the fracture and fold it twice or three times, removing any excess material. Then wrap the leg so that it reaches the ankle and thigh.
Then insert a long strip of adhesive tape between the skin and the plaster cast near the knee joint. The ends of the tape should come out above the two parts of the fracture and then wind them around the plaster cast and leg to remove any excess bandage material.
Then place a splint on the skin over the location of that fracture, and attach it with a safety pin to the bone of the plaster cast.
Then fill in the spaces between the plaster cast and the skin with soft cloths, so that the bandage can set properly.
And then start with a firm bandage on the middle of the fracture location with three or four turns. The more you wrap around the fracture site, the tighter and stronger the plaster cast will be. But also make sure it is not too tight or too loose, but rather comfortable and moist.
Then extend both ends of the adhesive tape to the foot and ankle on either side until it reaches the heel of the foot. Then fasten both ends of the safety pin to prevent any movement or slipping of the splint from its position.
Then leave the plaster cast on until it sets properly, making sure that there is no rubbing or irritation of the skin, nor any inflammation, swelling, or redness. If any of these symptoms do occur, then remove the plaster cast immediately and correct whatever has caused them, following the steps outlined above several times.
If there is any pressure on the bone that causes pain during movement, it is important to try and relieve that pressure as much as possible. If this is not possible, then place a pad over that area and remove any excess plaster cast material that may be causing discomfort. Then follow the steps outlined previously for treating a fracture until it has healed properly.
Sometimes the plaster cast on the thigh may cause pressure on the fracture site. If this happens, then make sure to adjust the safety pins on both sides of the plaster cast as described in the previous instructions for treating a fractured arm.
However, when treating a fracture in this way, it is important not to cause any limp or hobbling. If the plaster cast is applied incorrectly and the patient has trouble walking after the treatment, then it may be necessary to apply another plaster cast on top of the first one to help stabilize the leg during rehabilitation.
You should know that the plaster cast remains on for about 40 days or a bit more or less depending on the complexity and other factors of the fracture.
Chapter XV
On the treatment of fractures of the jawbone.
You should know that fractures of the jawbone are quite common and often occur due to trauma or blows to the face.
"Yet if it happens that he has a fracture, then it is but a crack or break in his bones, and this is accompanied by pain or no pain, upon which all depends."
"And the restoration of it is attempted by placing that which is broken of his bones with the said intestines being united and joined together as much as possible, according to what is easy, simple, and convenient."
"And then apply plaster and dressing over that round stone wound if necessary; and strengthen it with a bandage according to what is appropriate."
"And consider all the methods which we have recommended in other fractures, thus as with caution and similar care to those, so that it may be applied to every accident according to what is convenient for him, until he is healed."
"Chapter XVI."
"On the restoration of a fracture of the humerus."
"You should know that in the arm there are two bones of which one is thick and is called by the name of the arm, and the other is slender and is called the radius."
"And it happens that in some species of fracture the two bones of the arm are affected, and for that reason restoration is made of it just like restoration of the arm altogether, and the operation of it is one."
"And if the two bones are broken together, the humerus becomes completely disjointed."
"And if only the slender bone is broken, the arm remains articulated at the front."
"And if only the thick bone is broken, it remains attached to that of below and it is one of those causes which are not easily restored."
"And therefore apply extension, traction, and bandaging of the fragments according to what is appropriate in this case."
"But it happens that if the fracture of the humerus is old and there have been many attempts, then extension should be less and gentler, and alleviate with your skill."
"And in the humerus there is one cause for operation on the arm, and this is when you uncover the fragments and complete all your operation, then you will take pieces of wood from the trunk of a tree which has been placed below the causes which are placed beneath it, and made similarly from branches of palm; choose those which have little thickness, and let them not be thick ones, and let their length be according to the length of the humerus, and from one end to the other."
"And then apply on each splint two wrappings according to its length and place one on the fracture of the humerus on one side and the other on the other side, and they should extend from one end to the other."
"And then bind the two fragments in three places at both extremities and in the middle, because with this structure the humerus is prevented from bending to the right or left, and this is good stability."
"And then apply a splint of wood according to the length of the humerus, and let it be placed on it so that it conserves its movement."
"And more importantly you should do this in fracture, especially when it is properly reduced."
"And also consider each day the humerus and study it carefully, and you guard it from hematoma and inflammation or other similar causes. And whenever any of these occurs, then apply what is necessary until it is healed."
"Chapter XVII."
Of the fracture of the heel bone and bones of the foot.
The heel bone has not occurred fracture at all; but fractures of the bones of the foot have happened some times; and those of the foot rarely happen, and do not occur except attrition in the joints of position.
And if fractures happen to the bones of the foot and some of these bones become elevated above themselves among them, then the patient places their foot on the ground prepared in such a way that they can walk.
And around they pause the one foot on what is elevated of those bones, and around they press them, compressing and squeezing them until it returns to its place.
And around they apply plaster and bandages on top, and place under the belly of the foot a small table with two flat heads.
And around they stretch that member with the foot downwards, because thou hast enveloped it with cloths and for other reasons which thou shalt indicate.
And when three or four days have passed, then thou dost untie the bandage, for thou shalt find the bones squeezed together, or pinched, or dislocated, and the operation in those is thus known.
But if some of them are pinched together, then thou dost restore them and squeeze them thus with oil, and recoat them according to what thou shalt learn in the restoration of these bones of the hand. And around thou dost place the splint on the pinched finger according to its length, and let it be thus on one side a little; and around thou dost place under the foot that table which is recommended to thee, and stretch that member downwards.
But if what is pinched of the bones are two or three or more, then thou dost place on each finger separately according to its quantity, wrapped in lion's cloth, and stretch the table under the foot as far down as possible, and let it stand on one of the heads of the table with the belly of the foot upon it, so that it may have good retention.
And it is necessary for thee that thou dost not forget to not consider all the causes which are mentioned to thee in other fractures of accidents which we have said, and thou dost pause for each accident according to what is appropriate.
Chapter XVIII
Of the fracture of the neck of the woman and of the penis bone and of the phallus of man.
When the neck of the woman is pinched together, then thou dost perform that cut in this manner, and thou dost incline it at the joint of the two a little backwards.
And then the midwife presses the neck with cotton, and let it be done in the neck thus as an arrow; and then the woman is shaken up, and thou dost raise her hips little by little, for all this is forgotten until the neck is before the joint of the bone as an arrow, and the pinched bone returns.
And then thou dost place powder on the hips, and when she wishes to urinate thou dost remove the cotton with ease and let her urinate, and thou dost reduce it and return it according to the manner in which thou didst first introduce it, and then thou dost return it to its first powder.
And she shall do thus for seven days or thereabouts, until that is restored.
If you wish, take an ovine bladder and stretch it over the wound of the patient using a reed cannula; then insert the entire bladder into the concavity of the wound, and immediately inflate it with breath through the cannula so that the bladder becomes inflated within the concavity, for the fracture returns.
And then it should be filled with cotton and left in place for a few days according to what we have recommended, until it is healed.
When the bone of the penis in a man or woman is broken, at that time administer to its restoration and suturing the liquid that we have recommended for the hip bone.
And that which is right is not hidden in these nearby fractures and little endeavors, especially from him to whom some study and use of this my book are given; for that fracture indicates to you the greatest dispositions towards a straight restoration and tightening of it, therefore understand well.
But in the case of a broken penis in a man, take a guaiacum gum, and place it inside the penis; then wrap it with cloth wrapping and leave it wrapped for three days or around that time, and it will be healed.
Chapter XIX
Of the restoration of fractures of the bones when they are dislocated.
It is convenient for him who has suffered a fracture with dislocation, and especially if the bone is large like that of the thigh or shoulder blade, etc., to perform a flebotomy at once if the conditions for flebotomy are present in him as we have said above.
And if the dislocation flows with blood, it is necessary that you attend to its retention in such a way that you powderize over it zeugitris cut, if there is no other cause.
And also begin to restore the fracture on that same day, and do not delay it unless an abscess occurs.
But if an abscess occurs, then you should delay the restoration of it until the ninth day, until the abscess has healed, and do not approach it on the third or fourth day of all, for you will cause bad accidents.
But if the broken bone is prominent above the skin openly, then it is necessary for you to perforate and reduce it with your hands with ease and little extension.
But if extension is not prepared for you and the equating of it with your hands is difficult, then reduce it with this instrument; and this instrument is made of iron, its length being seven or eight digits and its width according to the quantity of the dislocation. And for that reason it is necessary for the physician to take three or four of them according to the quantity required in this cure in all kinds of fractures.
And let it be reduced, and let there be little hardness on it so that it is not doubled; then place pressure on it at the time of the operation.
And let there be an acute extremity on which margin is made in its extremities; and let it be thicker above than below, and larger inside the parts.
And this is its shape:
And it is called in Greek "labre volentz atelarem," that is, a little shovel.
For it must be paused on the elevated acute extremity of the bone on which the prominence is made.
And insert it in one stroke until the bone returns and is equated with some pressure.
And then force the ends of the fracture together with some equating, and if the end of the broken bone is thin and does not receive the instrument well, then insert the end of that bone until the instrument has power over it.
But if you cannot reduce the bone entirely with what we have recommended, then cut it with one of the knives which we have mentioned, or saw it with one of the saws in whatever manner is possible for you.
And then rasp that which remains on the bone of its sharpness and small rough edges.
And when the bone is reduced and the pain afterwards becomes very violent suddenly, then you should know that the bone has not returned to its natural place. And therefore do this in order that you may relieve the patient greatly.
When your reduction of the bone is completed, then immerse a cloth in black treacle and apply it properly if it is summer time, and do not put on the dislocation cerote or any other oil lest corruption and putrefaction occur later.
And apply plasters at the time of your completion of the reduction of the bone, and leave the dislocation uncovered enough that you insert the splint with forceps and use bandages according to the quantity of the dislocation.
And beware with all care that you do not stretch the dislocation with the fracture, for many times foolish physicians have done this and caused harm to their patients through death or consuming corruption or sudden death. Therefore be healthy. And if your splint is light, soft, different from the splints of other fractures.
If the inflammation is large or severe, and you fear any of the unfortunate accidents that we have mentioned, and find pain in the area, then do not place your hand on those stones, but instead use a wrap of hard cloth there and press it with those.
When the inflammation has begun to decrease after one or two days and pus has formed there, then remove the cloth that you had placed on it. Then apply compresses, using those with which we usually treat inflammations, such as tetrafarmacon and similar ones.
You should also remove the bandage and examine the wound every morning and evening until it has healed and is firm. And make sure that the limb is positioned in such a way that pus can flow from there to its lower parts with ease.
If several days have passed and the wound has not healed nor has the pus separated from it, then you should know that there is something wrong here. In this case, it is necessary to examine the wound with a tent (i.e., use a probe or exploratory incision). And of those things that are separated, remove and extract them. But if there are some that cannot be separated and they cause the limb to swell and give pain, then proceed to incise them according to what seems possible to you.
And if pus or ichor (blood) comes out of the wound, this is called stomatitis, or another type of corruption and putrefaction. In this case, you should pause for a moment and consider the care of that which has been described in its chapter.
And as to that in which my speech must be concise and you make your choice: when the bone is crushed, it rises above the limb just like the bone of the foot and helper bones, and similarly those of the large limbs. And do not attempt its attraction nor its extraction because many times death has occurred due to this; rather leave that alone until pus forms, for after about 20 or 30 days it will fall out on its own. Then treat the wound if you can see it, otherwise leave it alone.
Chapter XX
Of the treatment of swelling that occurs at the site of some fracture.
This swelling often occurs at the site of fracture healing, especially near joints, and distorts the shape of the limb. It often prevents the limb from functioning properly.
Then consider if the swelling is recent and apply to it medicines that have astringent properties like aloevera (aloe), sage, myrtle, rue, and similar ones. Take some or all of these and mix them with stiptic wine, egg albumin, or vinegar, then spread this on a piece of linen and bind it tightly over the swelling.
And let this dressing remain undisturbed for several days. Then remove it and apply another one until the swelling has been removed. Or place over that a lead plate (lamina of lead) descended in the middle, for lead has the property of reducing all swellings in the limbs.
But if the swelling has already become hard and it is necessary to remove it carefully, then cut it open along its upper surface, excise the excess tissue, or scrape it away with some curettes until it is removed. Then treat it until it heals.
De la curacio de fractura del cap.
"When a fracture has been restored and yet the limb remains fragile and does not follow its natural course."
"When a bone fracture has been healed, and yet the limb remains fragile, this may happen for various reasons. One of them is too much solution in the liquid and ligaments, which do not follow what is necessary; or due to an excess of tension in the ligaments, preventing nourishment from reaching the limb; or due to excessive embrocation; or due to movement at a time other than its usual one; or due to lack of blood in the patient's body and weakness."
"To cure this, feed the patient and make their heart tender so that blood may be multiplied in them; administer baths; and apply gauze and ointment on it."
"And especially avoid applying pressure on the limb, so that the pressure does not draw away nourishment from it; or continue rubbing it with warm water mixed with pitch, until nutrition returns to its natural state."
"Chapter XXII:"
"Of the cure of broken bones that have healed crookedly, and how to operate on them if necessary."
"When it happens to a limb that has already healed crookedly and unevenly at the site of the fracture and node formation, even though the limb is not entirely useless in terms of its natural operation, do not accept the claim of someone who says that limb was recently fractured."
"But many foolish physicians and restorers have done this in our land, and this operation is most reprehensible, leading to great fear among those who bear its mark, which is deadly."
"But if the limpness and node formation are recent, then it is necessary to rub them with warm water mixed with herbs that soften, such as aleatory flowers, rue seeds, mallows, and similar ones; and to make a plaster with a softening plaster like dulled lead well-made; or to take the juice of rue seeds and mix it with egg yolk or olive oil, and apply it on them; or to take fatty figs and grind them with pigeon dung, and similar medicines that are called amulets for consolidation."
"And sometimes it is dissolved with persistent light friction, especially with the most humid ones, and movement of the limb should be administered at all times in every hour."
"But if the crookedness has already become hardened and turned into bone, and it is necessary to operate on it with iron, then it is necessary to cut off what is above it and dissolve the continuation of the bone, and incise whatever is superfluous from the node that has grown there with fine scissors, and facilitate movement in that limb with diligence and care."
"Chapter XXIII:"
"Of discourse on dislocation."
"Dislocation is the displacement of one of the joints of its location, which hinders movement and alters the shape of the limb, causing great pain and injury."
"So when a dislocation occurs, it is necessary to reduce it as soon as possible and not delay reducing it, for if it is delayed, the joint becomes inflamed and hardened, making the reduction of dislocation difficult."
"And for this reason it is not necessary to move or stretch in the hour of inflammation, because many times it brings about spasm and severe pains."
"But when this happens, it is necessary that you go to the surgery of the sick person and let him rest until the inflammation subsides, and then rub the limb with warm water and oil, and reduce it gently."
"And all limbs should be treated with that which brought about their dislocation."
"And I have already ordered the chapters on dislocation according to what has been stated in fracture; from the most superior part of the body down to the most inferior."
"Chapter XXIV:"
"Of the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw."
"So when both mandibles are dislocated but not displaced; and the dislocation of both is done in one of two ways: either they are moved slightly from their places and softened a little; or they are completely dislocated, until they are softened at the joint where saliva flows, so that he cannot have it nor close his mouth, and his tongue is wrapped in his throat."
"But when the dislocation of him is slight, then with most dispositions it returns by itself without any cause."
"However, if the dislocation is complete and severe, then gentle reduction should be administered promptly, without delay."
"Then, the minister should hold the patient's head, and the doctor should insert their finger(s) into the dislocation of the jaw inside the patient's mouth, either with one finger if the dislocation is on one side, or both fingers if it's on both sides. The others holding the patient's hands outside should support them evenly; then tell the patient to relax their jaw and let go of any tension from each side. The doctor will then gently reposition the jaw until it returns to its proper place."
"However, if reduction is difficult, especially when both jawbones are dislocated at once, then evaporation with boiled water and oil should be administered to facilitate easy reduction without delay."
"Once reduction is achieved and the mouth is closed but not yet softened, then apply a poultice of linen cloth with wax and rose oil on it. Ensure that it's well melted and softened to facilitate easy application."
"And have the patient sleep on their back with their head between two pillows to prevent them from turning their head either to the right or left side. Do not force them to eat anything until they feel better and their jaws are comfortable again."
"And with this treatment, it should be easy for the patient to open their mouth for eating, drinking and speaking until their jaw is fully healed."
"And if the reduction of two dislocated jawbones is difficult and they do not return to their proper places at once, then it often happens that fevers occur and there is frequent thirst. Sometimes the abdomen becomes quite distended, and sometimes vomiting occurs."
"And when this happens, you should know that it is a sign of great danger. Many times the patient dies within ten days."
