Chapter .xxvi
Of the reduction of dislocation of the muscle.
Know that the muscle is not dislocated except in three ways, one of which is that it is dislocated at the departure of the tendon from the bone.
And the second way is that it is dislocated at the upper part of the muscle, and this is rarely done; and it is not dislocated at the posterior parts by the shoulder; nor is it dislocated at the anterior parts by the nerves.
And more often it is dislocated and protruded at the lower parts near the tendon, especially in those with little flesh, it is easily dislocated; but in those with much flesh, it comes out with difficulty.
And sometimes it happens to some men that by a blow or fall, the muscle becomes inflamed with an incurable inflammation, and is caught in such a way that it has already been dislocated.
Therefore you must first prepare for this before you begin treating him.
And the dislocation of it is recognized when it is at the lower parts near the tendon; therefore you must distinguish between the dislocated muscle and the normal one, for there is a clear difference between them. You will find in the head of the muscle a concavity below the tendon, and the touch of the head of the muscle will be like that of a hen's egg, and the patient cannot put his hand to his ear, nor can he move it in any way.
If it is dislocated at the foot or above, you will immediately find a palpable touch, and it is not hidden.
And this dislocation can be easily reduced when it is fresh and the patient is a child.
And the reduction of it is that the practitioner elevates the patient's hand to the upper part; and with his thumb presses below the tendon of it, and elevates the junction with force upwards to its place, and the practitioner elevates the patient's hand, and extends it upwards; and then he presses it down again for it returns easily.
But if it does not return by what we have said, and many days have passed since the dislocation happened, then the patient must be bathed in hot water and administered a liniment that softens and lubricates like this: decoction of comfrey root, althea, fenugreek, and mallow in water. This should be given to drink.
And then the patient is propped up on his back and pressed down below the tendon of it until it is well engaged between traction and compression, and then the physician places his heel on the sphere (i.e. a round object), and elevates the head of the muscle with force, pulling the patient's hand so that it does not hinder from below, and another assistant holds the patient's head so that it does not hinder from below, for immediately it returns to its place.
And if you wish to reduce it in this way: have present a man who is longer than the patient and on the same side of him; let him place his muscle below the tendon of the dislocated one and elevate the tendon upwards while the patient is suspended in air, and another assistant pulls the patient's hand downwards from below his belly. But if the patient is light in weight, then another cause must be suspended from him to weigh him down, for the dislocation returns immediately.
And it is reduced easily in this way: place on the ground a long pole with a rounded head like a pestle, neither too thick nor too thin, and let the patient be placed upon it; then lay him down upon cloths, and let him stand along the length of the pole, and extend his arm below the other side; and stretch out his body from the other side with force, for the junction returns to its place with great speed.
And if it is difficult to reduce it by all the causes that we have mentioned, then use this cure: take a pole of which the length is two braccia, and the width four digits, and let its thickness be level with the tendon in this way:
"And place lion's skin cloth over the head to prevent contagion of the illness."
"And then pause the movement of the illness, and extend your hand or arm inside the joints, and let the hand rest on the helper's chest."
"And then place your arm on the rung of a ladder according to its width, and extend your hand inside it, and hang the other side from the other side, for the junction is almost complete."
"And when the insertion is completed in every possible way, then pause the trembling with wool of equal quantity; and now apply a prepared plaster of powdered myrrh mixed with albumen of eggs over all the muscle, so that it may be turned upwards, and then strengthen the trembling with appropriate bandage; and let the ligament be turned over the plaster, and the hand of him be attached to his neck, and let it be released, and do not move his hand for seven days."
"And the food of the patient should be paused little by little, until the member is strengthened, because it is easier to heal when it is lighter."
"And then gradually increase the food after seven or nine days and exercise the movement; and when it is firm and not softened anymore, then it is healed."
"But if the junction is displaced many times due to humidity that occurs in him or for other causes, then it is necessary to apply cautery with three traughts in that place, according to what was said before in the chapter of cautery."
"And if even so it is done and after seven days the ligament is loosened and the junction is not firm, and the plaster and bandage are reduced upon him several times, and still it is not firm and caught, and he cannot raise that arm above his head, then know that the nerves of him, which are at the head of the muscle, are either cut or stretched or softened, and know that the junction will never be fixed in its place."
"For the dislocation of that which is done, you must place the foot and knee or the buttocks behind, and the reduction is done with impulse and extension with the hands, until it returns."
"And then apply the other bandage to that one, and care for it until it is healed."
"And if it happens that there is heaviness in the member and slowness in its movement during recovery, then the patient should use baths many times until that heaviness is relieved and returns to its first nature."
"Chapter XXVII."
"On the treatment of dislocation of the hip."
"Do you know that the hip joint is dislocated with difficulty and returns to its place with difficulty? And when it is dislocated forward or backward, it cannot be concealed from you if you examine it visually or by touch according to any figure that may have been dislocated. When you reduce the dislocated hip into its place, this is clearly indicated to you because the joint is concave and cannot bend the arm nor reach itself with that muscle."
"And you should cut it and reduce the dislocation before an abscess appears because if an abscess appears, reducing it is difficult, and the wound will not heal completely. And especially when it is dislocated backwards because this type of dislocation is worse than all others, causing great pain, and often death ensues from it."
"And the reduction of it, when it is possible to reduce it, is that the hand should be extended with two hands, and the arm should be abducted, and the doctor's hands should be placed on the joint and around it, and he should push the joint back into place with the ulnar sides of his hands together, or with the edge of his palm so that it returns to its place."
"But if the dislocation is anterior, then it returns as follows: by flexing the hand on its own, strike the joint with the edge of your palm."
"And if the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then apply strong extension and great force."
"And it is that two assistants should extend the arm, and hold it strongly with two hands so that it is not moved back during extension."
"And moreover, let the arm be rotated medially throughout because a rolled-up bandage or cloth, long enough to wrap around the hand of the patient, should be used."
"And when the doctor touches the joint with his hand, he should anoint it with some oil so that it may facilitate the causes which help the lubricity of the joint with ease. And moreover, he should push the joint back into place with strong pressure until it returns."
"And moreover, the reduction of it should be maintained by placing pressure on it with a compress in which stypticum and caustic are mixed with albumen of egg white. And let it be fixed with descending suspensory bandage, and let the arm be suspended from the neck for some days."
"And moreover, it should be released. And then if the joint is formed in its place, then release the bandage and leave it alone."
"And if you see that the joint is not strong, then repeat the compress and bandage, and leave it alone for some days until it is extended. And moreover, release it."
"But if there is gravity towards the reduction of the joint and its position is in movement, then apply humidification in bath and gentle friction, compression until it is alleviated. Or else place a compress on the joint that is moistened with olive oil so that it may be a greasy matter. And moreover, let it be wrapped, and leave it alone over the bone during the day and night." "And then remove that compress and place the limb in a warm bath. And when it is swollen, then rub the joint with moderate friction, and again apply compresses moistened with olive oil once or twice or three times." "while in the bath until it becomes flexible."
"And if you wish, apply to it a moistened and heated cowhide with its border thickened and strap it on tightly. And do this several times until that is humidified and reduced to its proper position."
"Chapter XXVIII."
"Of the treatment of dislocation of the shoulder blade, that is of the joint between the arm and the hand."
"The shoulder blade of the hand sometimes becomes dislocated and its reduction is easy compared to other joints, but nevertheless it should be reduced as soon as possible so that it does not become inflamed or infected."
"And the reduction of the dislocation is this: place the joint of the hand of the patient on a table and have the minister extend the hand of that one. And the doctor should place his hand or palm over the prominence of the joint and push it until it returns to its proper place."
"But it should be considered whether the palm has already been opened to the front of the hand. If so, then have the patient place the back of his hand on a table for extension and reduction. And if the dislocation is preceding, then have the position of the patient's hand inward on the table so that the doctor's hand may fit over that prominence of the joint."
"And then if it returns quickly, that is good. And if not, then apply compresses moistened with oil to reduce the inflammation and leave it alone. And do not repeat this unless there are signs of reduction after several days."
"But the joint moves according to its tortuosity. And do not tell the patient any cause other than that the hand is weakened and cannot move for some reason. Then you should know that the nerves are pinched or torn, and then it is not in its proper place but it must be reduced with cautery near its entrance and strongly done several times until it regains strength."
"So when the dislocation is returned to its proper place, then apply compresses as we have recommended and let it be left alone for five days. And then release it and let it be left alone while exercising the hand of that one."
"But if there is loss of movement and some gravity occurs in it, then apply cold water compresses with friction several times until it is alleviated."
"Chapter XXIX."
"Of the treatment of dislocation of these (fingers)."
"Sometimes these are dislocated entirely."
"And when some of these fingers are dislocated on the back or front of the hand, then straighten the finger and reduce its dislocation with your thumb and index finger until it returns to place."
"And after that, support the head of the finger; suspend it from the part where it is dislocated for two days."
"And next suspend that one and extend him so that his right side may be equated in that same day."
"And when it is time, read this second account to him and do not cease to untie him during the day and exercise him with movements, and tie him at night; and do this for a few days until he is straight."
"And do the same if it is dislocated at the navel of the hand, and read this account to that part and make the first operation your initial action until he is healed."
"And similarly do this when other parts are dislocated."
"Chapter XXX."
"Of the cure of dislocation of the vertebrae of the back."
"When dislocation occurs in any of the vertebrae of the back or neck, and some of them are removed from their places, then there is no cure for it because death ensues."
"And the sign of this is that the evacuation of the sick person is involuntary and he cannot retain it; many times some members are softened on one side or both sides of the body, or both arms or one of them; or one vertebra is removed from its place, because many times they are removed and many times their removal is made in four directions."
"Therefore that one is already removed from the parts behind and it is called 'gelbozitat'."
"But the cure for this is to consider if the 'gelbozitat' occurs in childhood, because then there is no cure nor healing of it at all."
"But in those cases that occur by chance or by percussion or by similar causes, the Ancients have already spoken at length about the ways of cure with their words, which is the most profitable; but I have abbreviated that one because a little excused the excess of it, due to my proximity to my intention of exposition, with which I have exposed and formed this from that diverse instrument with which I will show this and explain it."
"And only that one is cured which occurs properly at the back with what I have mentioned."
"And it is that the sick person should lie on his face close to a wall and be extended with his hands alongside him so that his foot is not injured."
"And properly a straight stick should be placed on the ground at the end of the foot near the head of the sick person, and another stick on either side of his feet at the other end of the foot."
"And the minister should hold one stick firmly without letting it go out of place; and another minister should hold the other according to that form."
"And properly wrap the head of the sick person with soft cloths and tie them firmly; and extend one end of the cloth to the stick that is placed near his head and attach it there; and properly stretch another cloth over his hips on either side of him and place it below his thighs; and properly adjust all the cloths and attach them to the other stick that is placed below his feet."
"And properly each minister should extend the cloth to its place on the stick; and let not the two sticks and their cloths be moved from their places where they were placed, but yet not fixed as we have said."
"And the doctor should place his palms on the back with strength until it returns, or he should place one table there and properly adjust his feet on the table until it returns."
"But if it does not return by this method, then take a table whose width or length is about three brazas, and dig in the wall that we have said is near the sick person a place where one end of the table can be inserted." "And properly place the middle of the table on the back; and the doctor should place his feet on the other end and stretch well until the back is contracted and returns to its place."
"And if you wish, open with a turn using your hand; and it is that you should lie on the ground near the head of the sick person between the two sticks at the end of his foot, each stick being one palm long, and already done in each stick a groove where the cord can run through, and each should be firmly planted in the ground so that they are not moved."
"And let there be another round stick on which the cord is placed, and it should be turned in the two grooves of the two sticks."
"And at one end of it is a groove in which the stick is formed, whose length is one palm with what has been turned; and the other two sticks have the same."
"And properly stretch the cloths that you have stretched on the foot of the sick person in the groove that is placed near his head, and the cloths that you have stretched over his hips in the cord that is placed below his feet."
"And properly place one minister on each cord who should turn the cord with his hand; and the doctor should straighten the back as we have said."
"And properly after the back has returned and the place is straightened, then you should put dry plaster with alum of egg white, and also cotton; and properly place it on the plaster like a table whose width is about three fingers or around those, and its length is as much as takes up the place of the inflammation, and on top of the back."
"And properly tie it with cloths that we have mentioned, and use the sick person subtly nutritious food until he is healed."
"And if there is still some inflammation in the place at the end of healing, then medicines that soften and cleanse should be administered with the application of the table for a long time, which we have recommended; and already lead plate has been applied to that place."
"And sometimes inflammation occurs at the end of the spine of the two hips, which is caused by that dislocation; and it is that bone that already inflames so much that there should not be any pressure applied to it with this medicine, for strong force will cause your death."
Chapter xxxi.
Of the treatment of a dislocated hip joint.
Know that the joint of the hip and the joint of the thigh muscle should never be dislocated except in dislocation alone, and it does not happen as those that happen with other joints of small movement and concavity.
And the joint of the hip is dislocated in four ways: it is dislocated inwardly at its inner parts, or outwardly at its outer parts, or anteriorly at its front parts, or posteriorly at its rear parts.
It is most commonly dislocated inwardly and less often anteriorly or posteriorly.
A sign of its dislocation inwardly is that when you stretch the healthy leg against the sick leg, it becomes longer and the knee is more prominent than in the healthy one; the sick person cannot bend his foot towards the shin, and this place where the shin should be pressed against feels numb due to a blood blister having formed there.
The sign of that which happens with dislocation at its outer parts is that the symptoms of it are opposite to this symptom.
The sign of that which happens with dislocation at its front parts is that when the sick leg is stretched out completely, it does not bend again due to pain in the knee; and if he tries to go forward, he cannot do so; his urine is retained and his gait is limping, and his walking is done on the heel.
"He who has experienced dislocation of the posterior hip joint will not extend his leg nor bend it forward without grimacing; the thigh is shortened compared to the other, and the joint is softened, with the head of the bone displaced in that region."
"If you reduce the species of this dislocation by considering whether it is old and chronic, and if you then attempt reduction according to its position, but there is no improvement in that treatment, then further traction is not necessary for such a case."
"If in the case where the dislocation is recent and belongs to one of the four species of dislocation, you will have need to reduce the joint, extend the hip internally and externally, move it to the right and left, for often it returns quickly and no other treatment is necessary."
"If it does not return in this manner, then you must prepare a powerful traction and extend the thigh from its outer side, either by itself or by a band which is tied above the hip joint; and another assistant should extend the arm of that person upwards so that they may grasp his toes with their hands; and finally, strongly pull the band downwards over the sacrum, while another assistant holds the end of the band."
"And let this extension be from the outer side to the angle at the head of the femur, or from behind to the trochanter."
"And let this extension be done all at once while the patient is lying on their back and holding their breath."
"And this type of traction is common to all four species."
"Therefore, if the dislocation returns with what we have said, it is good. But if not, then according to its own nature it will be necessary to apply the specific treatment for each species."
"In the reduction of its own kind, when it is dislocated and the dislocation is towards the inside, then let the patient be placed on their left side. Then place the band over the sacrum at that point where it lies between the head of the femur and the point which is lateral to the greater trochanter. And extend the band upwards from the side of the hip joint to its upper limit."
"And then another assistant shall strongly grasp the thighs of the patient and gently pull on the dislocation towards its outer side with a sudden extension, for that will return it to its proper place."
"And this is the easiest method by which this member can be reduced compared to other species."
"But if it remains on its outer side and does not obey the entrance with this method alone, then both hips of the patient must be tied together with a strong, light band placed above both ankles and knees."
"And let the length of each be four digits, and let the band be extended more on the side of the dislocation by two digits."
"And then have the patient suspended from a beam overhead and let him hang downwards. And let the length of it from the ground be two cubits."
"And then command a strong youth to grasp the head of the thighbone and let him suspend himself with the patient. And let another youth forcibly push on the thighbone, for the joint will quickly return to its proper place."
"But in the reduction of its own kind, when it is dislocated outwards, let the patient lie on their stomach as we have recommended previously. And let the band be placed properly over the side of the dislocation and over the foot."
"And then two beams shall be placed, one at the foot of the patient and the other at the head. And another beam fitted to the middle of the thigh shall be placed upon which wet cloths are wrapped so that the patient is not hurt by it being between his thighs, for otherwise the extension towards its outer side would be impaired."
"And then each assistant shall extend their respective parts and the physician with his hand shall guide the reduction according to the art."
"But when the dislocation is towards its front, then let the limb be gently extended and this is how it is done on the thigh. And the physician places his right palm over the hip joint of the patient, and then grasping that with one hand he extends it outwards to the point where it joins the knee."
"But when the dislocation is towards its back, then let not the limb be extended outwards and let it be raised up from the ground. For it should rest on a hard surface, just as it should if its ankle were dislocated outwards, according to what we have said concerning that joint resting on two ankles; and thus he lies on his face with the bandages extended as we have described."
"And compression must be administered by placing a board upon the place where the joint is dislocated."
"Therefore, when the reduction is completed as it should be - and the sign of its reduction will not be hidden from you, which is when you extend both thighs of the patient, and when you move those joints and the patient flexes his thigh and expands it without difficulty, then you shall know that the limb has been returned properly. And then you shall apply two splints and bind them with a bandage so that the ankle does not move in any direction; and keep the patient at rest for three or four days."
"And then untie the bandages and splints and compare one thigh with the other."
"Therefore, if you move those joints freely, then you shall know that the dislocation is healed, and then let the patient go."
"But if you see any sign of laxity, then return and splint it again, and extend it according to your first method for three days."
"And then untie that and let the patient walk slowly for a few days until he is strengthened."
"Chapter XXXII"
"Of the treatment of dislocation of the knee joint."
"The knee joint is dislocated in three ways: it can be dislocated outwards, inwards, and outwards more than backwards; but it cannot be dislocated forwards."
"The sign of dislocation of the knee joint is that when you tell the patient to apply their thigh against it, they know that the knee joint is dislocated."
"And the reduction of all kinds of its dislocation is that you make the patient lie on their right side so that they can extend their thigh if possible. Then adhere your two hands to his face and place his foot between your feet. And then press his knee joint with your palms, retaining it there between your fingers with force, and another assistant shall extend his foot until the knee joint returns to its place."
"The sign of reduction of the knee joint is that his thigh adheres to it with ease and without difficulty."
"And then splint that and make the thigh adhere to it. And then bind them together with a bandage for three or four days, and then untie them."
"And let not the patient walk for several days until he is healed and strengthened."
"But if reduction of it to you is difficult, then apply strong extension with the bandages mentioned in the previous chapter on the treatment of hip dislocation."
"Chapter 33."
"About the treatment of ankle dislocation."
The ankle sometimes becomes displaced by slight injury and sometimes becomes dislocated entirely; this dislocation occurs either inwards or outwards.
And the sign of ankle dislocation is that it often swells at the part where it has been dislocated.
But the treatment of ankle dislocation is easy: namely, that it should be gently reduced and returned to its place without difficulty.
But in the treatment of ankle dislocation when it is entirely dislocated, one should make the patient lie on their back and have someone strong and capable press on the middle part of the heel from behind.
And thus you should hold with your right hand on the upper part of the heel and with your left hand on the lower part near the ankle bone.
And then pull the heel towards you with your right hand and press on it twice with your left hand, and extend it with your left hand; then push the sole of the foot up against your knee without any effort.
And perform this twice as we have said before
Then push the sole of the foot up against your knee for the third time and you are at the ankle bone.
Therefore if the foot returns in one or two attempts by this method, and if the foot swells, that is good; but if not, then repeat the operation until the foot returns.
But if you are unable to perform the reduction of the foot by this method that we have recommended, then make the patient lie on their back on the ground and take a strong stick and place it between the toes of the affected foot, binding the end with cloth so that the foot does not slip.
And then the assistant should hold the patient's toes, and another assistant should extend the foot with their hands or with a bandage placed around the heel of the foot; and each assistant should extend against extension of his own part, while keeping the stick extended between the toes of the affected foot, so that the skin of the foot is not drawn towards its outer parts during extension.
And then the physician reduces the dislocation with his hands, while another assistant holds the healthy foot at its outer parts because the dislocation returns easily.
And when the dislocation returns and you confirm that the reduction is complete, then apply plasters and bind the foot strongly with bandages, and tie it to the knee.
And be careful or guard against the nerves which are located behind the heel bone, because if you bind too tightly in that area it may cause pain and injury.
Then leave him for two days or three; and if the bandage becomes damp, change it.
"And on the third day or fourth, untie it and forbid the patient from consuming wine for 40 days." "But if it is necessary to go before that space, there is no guarantee that the dislocation will not be dissolved and corrupted, especially because it does not receive treatment."
"And if an abscess occurs from the cautery, then you must apply to its cavity what was described earlier in this narrative, namely another place for cure and embrocation, until it is removed."
"Chapter XXXIV."
"On the treatment of dislocation of the said hips."
"It is necessary that one who has been dislocated be laid down gently with little extension without moving him; and that it should not be difficult, bending his knees slightly."
"But if the dislocation is in some of the joints of the thighbone, then you should cause the patient to sit with his bad leg stretched out on the ground or on a table, and he should remain thus as if walking."
"And then you should stand on one leg, and place your thighbone on what seems like the joints, and then press it with your foot with force until it returns to its proper position. Be careful that it has returned correctly and does not appear at the prominence of the joint."
"And then place the thigh of that leg on a table that takes up all the leg, with two ends, and stretch it moderately and firmly for three days; then remove the splint and let him rest from walking for several more days until he is strengthened and his recovery is assured."
"Chapter XXXV."
"On the types of dislocation that are done with pleasure or with fracture or each one separately."
"When some of these causes occur, and you strive to treat the dislocation and restore it, often you will meet with death."
"And therefore it is not fitting that anyone should presume on the treatment of such cases, but rather one who is skilled in the art, experienced, merciful and compassionate, careful, not hasty or presumptuous; and let him use medicines at first that will only draw out putrid matter, and leave the patient to divine judgment, not hoping for health from destruction, but rather alleviation of the disease, where there appears some hope." "And therefore strive at once to reduce it at the beginning of the cause before putrid matter forms."
"And then if the limb returns as we desire, then administer regimen that will draw out putrid matter and cure the inflammation with what is suitable for him of dry remedies."
"And if dislocation occurs with fracture and the bones are separated, then strive to reduce them, doing what we have said in simple cases that precede this."
"And guard and avoid entering into the paths of fear according to what has been preceded by my prediction; for this is better for you that you owe your Creator, and more lasting for your glory, and more beneficial for your true body in its time." "So end the words of Albucasis, may God bless them perfectly." "Amen."
